Old Man Knoppix might be your best friend once again.  You'd be better
off running a fsck when the root filesystem hasn't been involved in the
bootup.  Booting from a CD is ideal for situations like these.  Plus,
the version of fsck will be newer (if your knoppix disk isn't as old as
your RH7 load).

It's been a looonnng time since I used ext2, but I seem to remember that
you have to pass a switch to get verbose output.

Carlos Konstanski

From johnxj at comcast.net  Mon Nov  6 18:35:55 2006
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Mon, 6 Nov 2006 18:35:55 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Re: SQL Syntax
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0611061318380.18712@blackbox.fnordora.org>
References: <86d580q5gw.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
	<602E70A3AE4B924788440D1F5D572CC779D40E@glsuite01.c2msnet.local>
	<86zmb4h697.fsf@blue.stonehenge.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0611061318380.18712@blackbox.fnordora.org>
Message-ID: <20061106183555.e40e22bb.johnxj@comcast.net>

I DID IT!!!!

I've spent nearly two whole days on this project, which I thought might
take two or three hours when I started it. :(  Lots of learning
required, mostly figuring out OOo.

To refresh memories, I'm trying to create an editable PDF using OOo
Writer form controls. I have created a test document with drop-down
combo boxes, check boxes, radio buttons, and a text box. The drop-down
combo boxes are tied to various fields in a database, and that's where
one of the maddening problems cropped up. I couldn't get one of the
fields to display in properly sorted order. I spent hours trying to
figure out why -- the order in which records appeared was random. The
records in this field are IPA characters, mostly just one character. At
first I thought it might be sorting on Unicode #. But half an hour of
checking revealed that it was not in decimal or hex order (by Unicode
decimal or hex number). 

I tried all kinds of SQL statements, and all kinds of fiddling with the
GUI pop properties window for the controls, but nothing worked. 

Finally I noticed that in the GUI Properties box for "Type of list
content" it offered "table," "tablefields," "query," "sql," and
"sql-native." You're supposed to select one of these, then enter the
syntax in the box immediately below it. I thought I had tried them all,
but evidently I had not tried "query." I did so, and the box below
suddenly developed a drop-down button. But nothing was listed in it.
Thinking quickly, I realized that the database window I had open
elsewhere on the desktop had a button to display queries. I went there
and discovered there were options to create a query by "design view" or
by SQL statement (kinda sick of SQL right now). I clicked on design
view and up popped a window that I swear the OOo people stole lock,
stock and barrel from Microsoft Access. Now I was on familiar
territory! If you haven't seen these little GUI query design windows,
they are way easy for a non-programmer to create queries. Basically,
the window is a front end to create an SQL query. (In fact, in Access
you can toggle back and forth from design view to SQL view.) I should
also add that if OOo did some robbing, it's fair, because Microsoft
stole the idea from Borland, who called it Query By Example, and made
it a top selling point for Paradox.

I quickly selected my IPA field in the first column, and the Numbers
field in the second. I hit the checkbox for the Numbers field to make
it not visible, and then I hit the drop-down to tell the query to sort
ascending on the Numbers table. I saved my query, and went back to the
control properties popup window. Lo and behold, "Query1" showed up as an
option. I selected it, saved the form, and hit the button to return to
user view. Then I clicked on the drop down button for the control I had
changed and -- there were all my IPA characters sorted in the proper
order! Yay!!!

I don't know why I didn't find that query design thingy before, but now
that I know it's there, making other forms will be much easier. I've
written queries for all the other controls and fixed all of them. The
form is working perfectly now!

However, I still have one show-stopper problem remaining. When I export
to PDF the font I set the controls to is not getting embedded. My last
hope for that is GhostScript. If I can't get GhostScript to create the
PDF form correctly, I'll have to buy Acrobat 7 or 8, install it on my
Windows desktop, and move this operation there. Gack. I want this to be
a Linux-only thing.

Much thanks to all who helped earlier with the SQL stuff!

hey all,

How small can you make a Gentoo installation? I want to roll my own 
firewall/WAP/router Linux, and I want it to be as small as possible, and to 
be easily customizable and upgradeable. Which sounds like Gentoo, does it not? 
Pyramid Linux, Pebble, and other WAP Linuxes weigh in from 50-128 megabytes. 
How does Gentoo compare? Here's a sample feature list:

-iptables
-openvpn
-support only for installed devices
-openssh
-stunnel
-dnsmasq
-maybe some kind of public WAP frontend like Wifidog or nocat auth
-ntpd
-the usual gnu tools and essential system administration tools, some 
net tools

I want to keep it under 64 megabytes.

thanks!

Gentoo will never be a small-footprint distro because of the portage
tree.  I put a minimal installation on a 3.2GB hard drive once. It fit
until I added X and mozilla.  That put it over the top.  A similar
debian box could probably squeeze into a gig and a half or less.

I suppose you could get it built up and then delete all the portage
directories.  As long as you didn't run any emerge commands, it might
not blow up.  But if you forgot a package and had to emerge it, you'd be
in for a bummer time.

It's hard to imagine any desktop distro going this small.  What about
busybox?  Gentoo has busybox embedded in it, probably for bootstraping
purposes.  It comes on practically every consumer appliance router.

Carlos Konstanski

it can't because it needs (like any source distro, see e.g. others like lunar-linux.org 
or sourcemage.org) gcc and a rather complete set of headers and tools.

Anything under 500mb (not gentoo, but a really lightweight source distro like lunar) is 
an accomplishment, allthough these source distro's are rather easy to minimize and 
maintain manually (compile on a remote machine, and install sort of slackware-ish.... 
hey slackware, now that was a small distro).

If you really want to have a single maintainable source distro, you'd be looking at 
about 400mb minimal easily. Anything under that is pure occupational therapy.

Cheers

Auke (that other source distro guy)

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Mon Nov  6 21:47:10 2006
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Mon, 6 Nov 2006 21:47:10 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Error reading block xxxx
In-Reply-To: <454FCDB5.8050903@comcast.net>
References: <454FCDB5.8050903@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <20061107054710.GA32502@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Mon, Nov 06, 2006 at 05:05:09PM -0700, Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> We went away for a week, so I shut down all the hardware in the house.
> Upon our return, most of it started back up. My old 200 MHz machine
> running RH 7.0 reports a problem. After saying something to the effect
> that the filesystem hadn't been checked in a very long time (this
> machine ordinarily stays running) I got the following:
> 
> --------------------------------------------------------------------
> Error reading block 257756 (Attempt to read block from filesystem
> resulted in short read) while reading indirect blocks of inode 98117.
> NEC40GB: UNEXPECTED INCONSISTENCY; RUN fsck MANUALLY (i.e., without -a
> or -p options)
> --------------------------------------------------------------------

This is probably a flaw on the media surface that showed up over
your vacation.  If there are some marginal disk blocks that are
constantly being re-written on the disk, they may unwrite themselves
if the disk is idle long enough.  The fsck badblocks commands that
others talk about should fix it. 

However, you should be aware that leaving a machine on for a long
long time, then turning it off for a couple of weeks, then turning
it on, can sometimes uncover hardware failures.  For example, many
chips are covered with plastic, and can get very hot while running. 
This can cause the plastic to deform over time, opening up gaps. 
The hot chips tend to evaporate moisture in the gaps, but if the
room gets cool and damp over the turnoff time, moisture creeps into
the gaps in the chip plastic and causes failures.  The failures will
happen, someday, anyway.  They just tend to exhibit themselves
during the turn-on after an absence.  

The bottom line is, if you turn your machines off for a couple of
weeks here in the NorthWet, allow some time to repair them afterwards.
By all means, turn them off when you aren't using them, leaving them
on means they will fail sometime during use instead.  Just budget the
time for restores from backups or replacement of some hardware.  If
everything works, get some extra sleep!

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

Date: Mon, 6 Nov 2006 23:51:25 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] how small will Gentoo go?
In-Reply-To: <200611062044.20684.carla@bratgrrl.com>
References: <200611062044.20684.carla@bratgrrl.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.61.0611062343570.2130@shaft.bitmine.net>


On Mon, 6 Nov 2006, Carla Schroder wrote:
> How small can you make a Gentoo installation? I want to roll my own 
> firewall/WAP/router Linux, and I want it to be as small as possible, and 
> to be easily customizable and upgradeable. Which sounds like Gentoo, 
> does it not?

No, it doesn't.  Gentoo is not small at all and, while not particularly 
difficult to upgrade, wouldn't be in the list of "easiest" distributions 
to manage.

If you're not happy with any of the existing tiny distributions, you might 
consider (as I suggested before) setting up a subdirectory on your box and 
putting in only exactly what you want.  You can test it by making a chroot 
environment or UML and running your programs inside.  After you've 
determined exactly what you want in terms of applications, you can start 
over again with source and build only those libraries and utilities that 
you need with only the functionality you need.  Strip all the executables 
and you might have something.

To me, it seems like an enormous hassle for very little gain.  You can 
build a business card-sized Debian system that is apt-upgradeable and 
quite reasonably small with almost none of that other effort.  Do a bare 
install and deselect everything but the required base packages and then 
just add one package at a time until you have what you want.  Should be 
well within the size range of cheap flash memory.

J.
-- 
    -----------------
      Jeme A Brelin
     jeme at brelin.net
    -----------------

On 11/6/06, Carla Schroder <carla at bratgrrl.com> wrote:
> hey all,
>
> How small can you make a Gentoo installation? I want to roll my own
> firewall/WAP/router Linux, and I want it to be as small as possible, and to
> be easily customizable and upgradeable. Which sounds like Gentoo, does it not?
> Pyramid Linux, Pebble, and other WAP Linuxes weigh in from 50-128 megabytes.
> How does Gentoo compare? Here's a sample feature list:
>
> -iptables
> -openvpn
> -support only for installed devices
> -openssh
> -stunnel
> -dnsmasq
> -maybe some kind of public WAP frontend like Wifidog or nocat auth
> -ntpd
> -the usual gnu tools and essential system administration tools, some
> net tools
>
> I want to keep it under 64 megabytes.
>

Like others have said, gcc, the associated libraries, and utilities
for building things bumps
this up way too high.  Now you could host your build and distro on
anything and just
export the resultant code without package management.
Here are some build systems that I would look at.
buildroot
openwrt or DD-wrt

>>>>> "Carla" == Carla Schroder <carla at bratgrrl.com> writes:

Carla> -maybe some kind of public WAP frontend like Wifidog or nocat
Carla> auth

Just a warning, nocatauth depends on perl, so it probably isn't a good
choice for a small install.  Nocatsplash is small, C-based and
self-contained.  PTP is considering migrating to wifidog, though it
behaves a little differently than we are used to.

And frankly, I think you'd be better off just getting comfortable with
pyramid or pebble or one of those.  Stand on the shoulders of, er,
taller, less-lazy people.  Adding packages to pyramid from ubuntu is
pretty easy.  I added bridgeutils at one point and it took about 15
minutes to finish, bumps-and-all (damn you to hell, libsysfs!)  You
can build a chroot environment that makes building from source easy
and fast (on your desktop hardware), then copy to your image and away
you go.


-- 
Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net


From sofar at foo-projects.org  Tue Nov  7 07:47:51 2006
From: sofar at foo-projects.org (Auke Kok)
Date: Tue, 07 Nov 2006 07:47:51 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] how small will Gentoo go?
In-Reply-To: <200611070047.02619.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
References: <200611062044.20684.carla@bratgrrl.com>	<Pine.LNX.4.61.0611062343570.2130@shaft.bitmine.net>
	<200611070047.02619.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
Message-ID: <4550AAA7.4050909@foo-projects.org>

Eric Wilhelm wrote:
> # from Jeme A Brelin
> # on Monday 06 November 2006 11:51 pm:
> 
>> If you're not happy with any of the existing tiny distributions, you
>> might consider (as I suggested before) setting up a subdirectory on
>> your box and putting in only exactly what you want.  You can test it
>> by making a chroot environment or UML and running your programs
>> inside.
> 
> Along this same train of thought, I wonder how small it would be if you 
> simply installed debian on a chroot with several throwaway mounts in 
> it?  /usr/include, /usr/share/man, and /usr/share/doc come to mind.  
> While it wouldn't be as small as possible, it would certainly be easy 
> to maintain (guess you could even have gcc and such if you installed 
> them in /usr/local -- and maybe it would even work for gentoo.) (hmm. 
> stow?)  Anyone seen this done before?

not worth the hassle. /usr/bin and /usr/lib (and the non-/usr/ brothers of those) are 
already quite huge on modest systems. On my box this accounts for 1.5gb, but even on the 
most minimal system I work with (our source distro's (lunar) installer ISO build), we 
are already at 102mb (!) [[the entire build system is about 360mb, of which about half 
is compressed packages]].

So much for being absolutely minimal.

If you really want minimal, the only real option still feasible for ordinary distro's is 
nfsroot or similar, and just move all requirements off to a different storage system.

Cheers,

Auke

On Mon, Nov 06, 2006 at 08:44:20PM -0800, Carla Schroder wrote:
> 
> How small can you make a Gentoo installation? I want to roll my own 
> firewall/WAP/router Linux, and I want it to be as small as possible, and to 
> be easily customizable and upgradeable. Which sounds like Gentoo, does it not?

Oh goody!  I get to be Santa Claus for Carla!

Because you've been Sooo GOOOD this year young lady, you get to find
out about EWRT, sponsored by our fine friends at Portless Networks.

http://www.portless.net/menu/ewrt/

Which, if you have one of the appropriate older versions of the Linksys
WRT54G or WRT54GS wireless access points, or one of the new ...GL versions,
you can crosscompile and upload into the unit.

Other options involve older versions of the Netgear WGT634U, which have
USB host support and allow you to add all sorts of other goodies.  That
busy elf, PLUG's own Russell Senior (russell mumble personaltelco mumble
net), built something called a bike stumbler which he has heavily hacked
to log wireless access points onto a USB key, with GPS information, while
he rides his bicycle around.  Way cool.  So there is more than one kind
of unit that you can do these hacks on.

So you can get it all done with a $50 WAP, and some specialized distros
designed to download into them.  If you want to add features that aren't
already in the extensive list of customizations these folks are working
with, you will at least be 95% of the way there.  And since the one thing
they are lacking is somebody to /write up/ their endeavors for mass
publication, you can be Santa Claus for them, too!

Keith

From ianburrell at gmail.com  Tue Nov  7 10:35:33 2006
From: ianburrell at gmail.com (Ian Burrell)
Date: Tue, 7 Nov 2006 10:35:33 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Xvfb experience
In-Reply-To: <454FCEF5.2070705@acm.org>
References: <454FCEF5.2070705@acm.org>
Message-ID: <d91f09cd0611071035x5430c101v2ea0c910e01c269e@mail.gmail.com>

On 11/6/06, Roderick A. Anderson <raanders at acm.org> wrote:
> Does anyone have experience using Xvfb?
>
> We have found a Windows application that does just what we need, has
> command line version and runs via Wine.
>
> It would be called by a Catalyst ( mod_perl ) application.  The problem
> is wine doesn't like not having $DISPLAY/ X Server and throws lots of
> error messages.  The application works fine but we don't need the cruft
> in the logs.  So the idea is we could use Xvfb.  Only problem is I've
> never used it.
>
> So the questions arise!  Can apache -- the user -- access 'X', and
> how/where do I start Xvfb?
>

I have used Xvfb for Java web apps which were using AWT graphics
before they introduced headless mode.

You start the server like this: "Xvfb :1". Then use :1 as the DISPLAY.
You can either start it before the app or as a separate service. It
uses the normal X security. I think that by default it doesn't use any
authentication so any local user can connect. If not, you can use
"xhost" to allow access.

 - Ian


 - Ian

Back when I used Windows 2000 I had Norton File Manager, which had FTP
capability. You could just put the FTP address in an entry and you
could drag and drop files to/from the FTP site. It would even prompt
for a password if necessary, and save the password if you wanted it to.

I need to set up a web site as a student at PSU. I've read the
instructions and it says to transfer by FTP to
odin.pdx.edu/~johj/public_html. Presumably that folder is already
there; created when I set up my Odin account a long time ago. 

I've created the website with OOo (really simple little website, just
needed for people to download a couple things). Now I need to FTP the
files to PSU. I tried Konqueror, but couldn't get it to work. Is there
a simple little GUI FTP transfer utility I can install that will make
these things easy for GUI-addicted folx? Or if it is easier by command
line, can someone give me a hint about what commands I need to learn?

On Tue, Nov 07, 2006 at 12:28:06PM -0800, John Jason Jordan wrote:
> I've created the website with OOo (really simple little website, just
> needed for people to download a couple things). Now I need to FTP the
> files to PSU. I tried Konqueror, but couldn't get it to work. Is there

Konqueror works fine with odin. Note that the OIT machines use
SFTP, not plain FTP. In the Konqueror location bar, you'd enter
"sftp://jjj at odin.pdx.edu/home/jjj/" (replacing "jjj" with whatever
your actual username is). It'll ask you for your password, and then
take you to your homedir.

From pm at nellump.net  Tue Nov  7 13:06:20 2006
From: pm at nellump.net (Paul Mullen)
Date: Tue, 7 Nov 2006 13:06:20 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] how small will Gentoo go?
In-Reply-To: <4550AAA7.4050909@foo-projects.org>
References: <200611062044.20684.carla@bratgrrl.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.61.0611062343570.2130@shaft.bitmine.net>
	<200611070047.02619.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
	<4550AAA7.4050909@foo-projects.org>
Message-ID: <20061107210620.GB1393@nellump.ath.cx>

On Tue, Nov 07, 2006 at 07:47:51AM -0800, Auke Kok wrote:
> If you really want minimal, the only real option still feasible for
> ordinary distro's is nfsroot or similar, and just move all
> requirements off to a different storage system.

If you really want minimal and not have to rely on a remote file
system, a common approach is to build a custom kernel image (with no
frills--nothing but what you need), and a basic complement of
libraries and utilities based on a pared-down glibc and BusyBox. Such
a system can easily fit in less than 4 MB of storage. It won't do all
that much, but it will qualify as a mostly full-fledged Linux system.
If you want to add other fancy stuff like servers and such, you'll
likely need more space.

http://www.busybox.net/

Check out the handhelds.org site for a good example of what can be
squeezed into a small amount of flash RAM (32 MB, in their case). They
host the Familiar Linux distribution, which is meant for PDAs such as
the iPaq.

http://www.handhelds.org/

For a turnkey cross-toolchain, I like the Embedded Linux Development
Kit (ELDK). It includes kernel source for various PowerPC and ARM
targets, and also ships with ramdisk images for both (meaning you
don't even have to bother building your own filesystem, just the
kernel image).

http://www.denx.de/wiki/DULK/ELDK

-- 
Paul

# from Auke Kok
# on Tuesday 07 November 2006 07:47 am:

>not worth the hassle. /usr/bin and /usr/lib (and the non-/usr/
> brothers of those) are already quite huge on modest systems. On my
> box this accounts for 1.5gb, but even on the most minimal system I
> work with (our source distro's (lunar) installer ISO build), we are
> already at 102mb (!) [[the entire build system is about 360mb, of
> which about half is compressed packages]].

Err, ok.  My debian webserver (no X) has them at 121MB, and that's with 
php, mysql, vim, subversion, gcc, and a bunch of other stuff that I was 
suggesting could be built in /usr/local and/or managed by juggling 
symlinks.  Maybe it's still too much hassle to get down to minimal that 
way, but it's definitely not 1.5GB from a bare install.

Maybe the debian packages could be rebuilt to put build-requires stuff 
in /usr/local and run-requires stuff in /usr/.  Possibly easier to take 
this approach with Gentoo.

You don't really need an nfsroot if you're "booting" for maintenance 
from a chroot with the fun maintenance stuff mounted in from elsewhere 
(or even mounted as-needed on a big usb drive on the production box 
(i.e. the maintenance environment could be shared amongst many wee 
boxen.)  Maybe nonsense.  Oh well.

From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Tue Nov  7 18:44:37 2006
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Tue, 7 Nov 2006 19:44:37 -0700 (MST)
Subject: [PLUG] lisp problem: switching packages on the fly
In-Reply-To: <86lkmoq60m.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0611052243230.12285@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>
	<86lkmoq60m.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0611071913220.30636@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>

> As one of the few PLUG advocates of Lisp, I feel that question is
> directed at me, so I'll try to respond.  I have never really learned
> how to make effective use of packages.  I once asked someone who was
> much more expert than I and had been the CTO of a company using Common
> Lisp in a more industrial way than I ever have (although, admittedly,
> he was a scheme guy at heart) and he said that they didn't use
> packages.  So, I didn't get much of a leg up on packages from that
> conversation.  I guess my advice is to read more about packages (in
> the Hyperspec, CLtL2, etc) and also ask on a Common Lisp forum like
> comp.lang.lisp.  And if you figure it out, let me know.
> -- 
> Russell Senior         ``I have nine fingers; you have ten.''
> seniorr at aracnet.com

Let's just say that I had you in mind as a hopeful, Russell.  You did
get me thinking that package manipulation was not necessarily the answer
for this problem.  You were right.  I guess the java programmer in me
was looking for a stack of nouns to throw at the problem, when a verb
was clearly needed.

Instead of trying to call the function directly, making it execute
within the :araneida-site package, and have it try to call back into the
place where I should have stayed all along, I do it the lisp way, which
is to fetch the function as data and run it in the context of the
:jewelry-site package.  This way, the compiler does not try to compile
the function as a top-level form, since it is just data.  Therefore, the
function can contain anything at all.  Its contents only get evaluated
at the moment the function is actually called.

(defpackage #:jewelry-site
   (:use #:cl
         #:araneida-site))

(in-package #:jewelry-site)

;; The data that is used by the shared function, but defined
;; locally here, in this child package
(defparameter skin-config `(:color-canvas "#539083"
                             :color-background "white"
                             :color-dark "black"
                             :color-medium "#779193"
                             :color-light "#dddddd"
                             :color-white "white"
                             :color-alink "blue"
                             :color-vlink "maroon"
                             :color-error "red"
                             :color-highlight "yellow"
                             :font-family "verdana, tahoma, helvetica, arial, sans"
                             :font-size-default "14px"
                             :font-size-text "12px"
                             :font-size-tinytext "10px"
                             :border "white 0px solid")
   "A list of all style values for the webapp.")

;; The code that calls the shared function, which itself uses the
;; data defined local to this package
(defun with-wrapping-html ( &rest body)
   "The template for most pages in the website.  Renders the banner,
banner buttons, left-hand menu, footer and javascript DHTML library."
   `(html
    (head
     (title ,(get-webapp-title))
      ((style)
       ,@(araneida-site:stylesheet-body)))   ;; The moment of truth!
    ((body)
     ...)))

;; ====== ;;

(defpackage #:araneida-site
   (:export #:stylesheet-body)
   (:use #:cl))

(in-package #:araneida-site)

;; The shared function that is not compiled until the moment it is
;; called.  Therefore, it can contain references to symbols that
;; are nowhere to be seen in the context of this package, as long as
;; it always gets called from within a supportive context.
(defun stylesheet-body (webapp-package)
   "Outputs the body of the stylesheet."
   #'(lambda ()    ;; The part that turns this function into a bunch of data
       `(,(concatenate 'string
"body {
     background-color: " (getf skin-config :color-canvas) ";
     color: " (getf skin-config :color-dark) ";
     font-family: " (getf skin-config :font-family) ";
     font-size: " (getf skin-config :font-size-default) ";
     font-weight: normal;
     scrollbar-arrow-color: " (getf skin-config :color-medium) ";
     scrollbar-track-color: " (getf skin-config :color-medium) ";
     scrollbar-highlight-color: " (getf skin-config :color-medium) ";
     scrollbar-darkshadow-color: " (getf skin-config :color-medium) ";
     scrollbar-shadow-color: " (getf skin-config :color-medium) ";
     scrollbar-face-color: " (getf skin-config :color-white) ";
     scrollbar-3dlight-color: " (getf skin-config :color-medium) ";
}"))))

Carlos Konstanski

Option #1 sounds dandy, except for the old fsck version factor.

There is another option.  Use a knoppix 4.0 disk.  I find that 5.0 is a
little bit sucky.  For one thing, I've seen it fail to boot on some
machines, where a 4.0 disk works.  Another problem with 5.0 is that it
automounts your hard drive partitions read-only.  Why did they decide
that was a good idea?

I cannot find a knoppix 4.0 iso for you, unfortunately.  I wonder if a
gentoo install disk might have the necessary tools.

Carlos Konstanski

   Alternatively, go to Powell's Tech Book store or the local library and get
a book that gives a generic background on linux. Not distribution-specific,
but an introduction to the philosophy and structure of the OS.

   Learn what filesystems are, how there are little tools to do specific
things (but thousands of those little tools), what is meant by multi-tasking
and multi-user, the differences between working on a console and within the
X Window System.

   An overview can make it a lot easier to understand the what and why.

To really be sure what drives your system can see and what they are
called, do an "fdisk -l".

Googling for "fsck device not configured" is coming up with scant
pickings, but none of them are overwhelmingly good.  The partition table
is suspect.

"Device not configured" must be a message thrown by a low- or kernel-level
task.  It comes up for all sorts of hardware problems.  Most likely, it
means that there is no kernel driver support for the device.  If you
have one IDE drive working and another one that doesn't, this
explanation doesn't jive.  Maybe the drive has become a total goner.

I'd get that knoppix 4.0 disk in there and see if a newer version of
fsck.ext2 will help.

Carlos Konstanski

Carlos Konstanski wrote:

In my readings of man fdisk I ran into a reference to /proc/partitions.
It lists hda and hdd. Then I remembered that the second drive is the
slave on the controller that also has the cdrom drive, hdc.

----------------------------------------------------------------------
[root at nec /proc]# cat partitions
major minor  #blocks  name     rio rmerge rsect ruse wio wmerge wsect
wuse running use aveq

   3     0    8420800 hda      26658 10483 295598 270910 6163 4084 82124
1261080 0 257320 1532330
   3     1     104391 hda1     39 204 486 470 6 3 20 1950 0 1800 2430
   3     2     265072 hda2     12 116 1018 240 153 1093 9968 13210 0
3580 13450
   3     3    8048565 hda3     26603 10157 294074 270170 6004 2988 72136
1245920 0 256410 1516420
  22    64   39082680 hdd      2 0 4 100 0 0 0 0 0 100 100
  22    65   39078081 hdd1     1 0 2 0 0 0 0 0 0 0 0
----------------------------------------------------------------------

The numbers for hdd don't seem encouraging compared to hda.

----------------------------------------------------------------------
[root at nec /dev]# fdisk -l hdd

Disk hdd: 255 heads, 63 sectors, 4865 cylinders
Units = cylinders of 16065 * 512 bytes

Device Boot    Start       End    Blocks   Id  System
  hdd1   *         1      4865  39078081   83  Linux
----------------------------------------------------------------------

This at least looks normal.

----------------------------------------------------------------------
[root at nec /dev]# e2fsck -c hdd
e2fsck 1.18, 11-Nov-1999 for EXT2 FS 0.5b, 95/08/09
Couldn't find ext2 superblock, trying backup blocks...
e2fsck: Bad magic number in super-block while trying to open hdd

The superblock could not be read or does not describe a correct ext2
filesystem.  If the device is valid and it really contains an ext2
filesystem (and not swap or ufs or something else), then the superblock
is corrupt, and you might try running e2fsck with an alternate superblock:
    e2fsck -b 8193 <device>
----------------------------------------------------------------------

I get the same results running e2fsck -b 8193.

> I'd get that knoppix 4.0 disk in there and see if a newer version of
> fsck.ext2 will help.

50:21 remaining. I hope you're right about the newer version helping.

Thanks for YOUR help.

And if you don't have time (or access in some cases) to a library, check
out the online documentation at "The Linux Documentation Project."  It's
available at http://www.tldp.org.

There's some really good (and not so good stuff) there.  Browse through
and see if there's anything that would be nice to start with.

I'm partial to NAG (The Network Administrator's Guide), SAG (the System
Administrator's Guide) and LAME (Linux Administration Made Easy).  But
they all expect a little bit of knowledge (and are a little dated).

There's also "Introduction to Linux - A Hands on Guide" but I haven't look
at it yet, and the "Bash Guide for Beginners."  Those are both at
http://www.tldp.org/guides.html

Welcome to the source...

On Wed, 8 Nov 2006, Richard C. Steffens wrote:

> Then I remembered that the second drive is the slave on the controller
> that also has the cdrom drive, hdc.

Dick,

   I don't recall the reason why this is a bad idea, but rejumper the drives
so the hard drive is the master and the cdrom drive is the slave. You -- and
the kernel -- will be accessing the hard drive much more frequently than the
cdrom drive.

   A number of years ago I had drives set up as you do and had some problems
with them (I don't recall just what they were). Swapping the master and
slave fixed everything.

Rich

On Thu, 9 Nov 2006 06:49:37 -0800 (PST)
Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> dijo:

> On Thu, 9 Nov 2006, GLL wrote:
> 
> > : And what the hell does "Capiscere" mean?
> 
> > Sounds lovely and very Western European / Latin / Classical. I like it. It
> > could be the name of a six-dollar confectioner's coffee, or maybe it's a
> > sub-minor diety from the Roman Pantheon; some forgotten girl in an Empire
> > waist sort of thing who polished and waxed the Halls of Olympus.
> 
>    I think it's the name for Starbucks' smallest cup of house coffee.

I love these responses. English speakers, especially, develop a "sense"
of what a word might mean even if they've never encountered it before.
The theory is that the many language collisions that English has
suffered has invested it with small morphs that "sound" like something.
Thus we combine and recombine them, frequently to the point where they
have lost all sense of their original meaning. :)

In any event, "capiscere" (pronounced with four syllables), is an
American made-up Italian verb, commonly used by pseudo cognoscenti
(more Italian), particularly in eastern metropolitan areas. It arose
from the real Italian verb "capire" which is derived from Latin
"capire," to take, grab, capture. Indeed, English "capture" is a Latin
borrowing from the same verb. In modern Italian "capire" has lost most
of its "capture" meaning and instead is the common verb for
"understand." Think of the English expression "Get it?" and you can see
how the meaning drifted from capturing to understanding.

The second person singular form of "capire" is "capisce," (sometimes
pronounced with an s and sometimes with an esh, depending on dialect.
So in places where there are concentrations of Italians, e.g., New York
City, English-only speakers kept hearing the question "Capisce?" and
eventually it was adopted into English as another way to say "Get it?"
And then the pseudo cognoscenti, who had studied enough French, Latin,
Spanish or Italian to understand vaguely how Romance verbs work,
invented a new infinitive form "capiscere."

And yes, I was in the meeting about mplayer. But I must have missed the
fact that it could convert. I'll fire it up right away and get those
evil WMAs converted to something more pleasant.

"virii" would be the plural of the mythical masculine noun "virius".
The neuter plural of "virus" would be "vira" (as "opus" & "opera"),
and "viri" is the plural of the masculine "vir" (man).
It really is "viruses"!  -- From Nick Moffitt's sig

because I live in the wilds of Eastern Oregon. There are no kewl local 
shoppes.) 

I ordered a Rosewill RX353-S SATA enclosure. It has a cooling fan, and comes 
with both USB and SATA cables. The SATA cabling includes an external rear 
bracket. 

I bought a Western Digital 250-gigabyte 3.0 SATA drive for the insides. Slides 
right in, no problem. It seemed a bit wobbly, so I stuffed some anti-static 
foam around the sides to pack it in more tightly.

Plugged it in, fired it up, made one big partition and formatted it in ext3. 
Copied over a gazillion giant digital photos without a hitch. I'll use the 
SATA connector and backup my LAN to this drive, the usual rsync blahblah. 
SATA speeds don't much matter for network backups, but it's connected to my 
photo-editing workstation, so those speeds will come in handy.

Easy peasey and fun!
-- 
~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
Carla Schroder
Linux geek and random computer tamer
check out my Linux Cookbook! best
book for sysadmins and power users
~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~

In-Reply-To: <455164F7.4090504@dslnorthwest.net>

Denis Heidtmann wrote:
> Paul Mullen wrote:
>> On Tue, Nov 07, 2006 at 07:51:41PM -0800, Denis Heidtmann wrote:
>>> Anybody have any words of wisdom?
>>
>> Have your son repeat the export operation? Kimdaba offers a few
>> different options on how to handle images during export (e.g., include
>> them in the .kim file, create a directory structure that sits
>> alongside the .kim file, etc.).
>>
>> What version of Kimdaba is he using? It's possible there's some
>> incompatibility if it's an older version. Note that Kimdaba changed
>> its name to KPhotoAlbum as of v2.2.
>>
> We are both using 2.1.  We can try again, but transferring the files 
> takes a very long time.  We can do some tests, short of sending the 
> entire collection. Good idea.
> 
> I have no way of knowing if 2.2 would help, and I cannot find a packaged 
> kPhotoAlbum.
> 
> -Denis

Well, I found out a few things, and in the process solved the problem.  Export 
without marking the check-box to include thumbnails produces, during import, an 
error window for every picture in the collection.  So after 926 "OK" clicks, the 
import proceeded.  Checking "include thumbnails" during export avoids this problem.

-Denis

/etc/network/interfaces:

          netmask 255.255.255.0

In brief-
We have a home-built computer that's been working well for the past 5 
years or so.  Yesterday, my spouse added a DIMM to it. It seemed ok, 
although we discovered that it spontaneously rebooted several hours later.
This morning, it is dead. No activity of any kind when the power is turned 
on, except the USB wireless adapter's LED glows, indicating that it is 
getting power. We removed the added DIMM. No change in behavior, still 
non-responsive.

Question: how do we start troubleshooting this?

Much obliged for any and all bits of help.

David Fleck
david.fleck at mchsi.com



From galens at seitzassoc.com  Sat Nov 11 07:40:58 2006
From: galens at seitzassoc.com (Galen Seitz)
Date: Sat, 11 Nov 2006 07:40:58 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] What's the best way to troubleshoot severe and sudden
	hardware death?
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0611110831280.5646@grond.sourballs.org>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0611110831280.5646@grond.sourballs.org>
Message-ID: <4555EF0A.7060900@seitzassoc.com>

David Fleck wrote:
> In brief-
> We have a home-built computer that's been working well for the past 5 
> years or so.  Yesterday, my spouse added a DIMM to it. It seemed ok, 
> although we discovered that it spontaneously rebooted several hours later.
> This morning, it is dead. No activity of any kind when the power is turned 
> on, except the USB wireless adapter's LED glows, indicating that it is 
> getting power. We removed the added DIMM. No change in behavior, still 
> non-responsive.
> 
> Question: how do we start troubleshooting this?

Are the fans spinning?

Do the LEDs on the keyboard flash?

Any beeps from the speaker?

If you have a voltmeter, check a disk power cable.  Yellow to black is 
+12V.  Red to black is +5V.

If you think power is good, you might try reseating the processor and 
DIMM.  Check to make sure all cables are properly seated.


galen

Power supplies have a safety breaker in them.  I've tripped them just by
touching the case with sufficient static charge built up on my person.
The breaker flips back after a lengthy period of time (half hour?), but
you have to unplug the power cord.

Carlos Konstanski

Sounds bad, though maybe the power supply's good and the mobo isn't

whole computer) in to somewhere like Pacific Solutions or the FreeGeek

I recently bought two new power supplies (on sale at newegg.com) because
I needed one and I knew I'd eventually need another because I have had
bad power supply karma lately.  Plus now I can give my old 'test' power
supplies to FreeGeek.

> Here's a new question: could simply adding in a stick of memory (256MB 
> PC-3200 400Mhz) fry a power supply? Or is that mere coincidence? This

I'm not really a hardware guy (though I desoldered the audio tower off a
motherboard yesterday and it still works), but my understanding is that a
system that has run in one configuration for a long time may not
tolerate changes well.

I've read that if you run a CPU at its rated speed for a few months,
the odds are against being able to overclock it much. By contrast, if
you overclock it from the start, your odds are much better. I'm not into
overclocking because my applications require reliability, but that seems
to suggest that hardware doesn't like change. 

Hardware wizards? Please enlighten us.

--Kurt

From mel97215 at comcast.net  Sat Nov 11 13:49:38 2006
From: mel97215 at comcast.net (Mel Andres)
Date: Sat, 11 Nov 2006 13:49:38 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] What's the best way to troubleshoot severe
	and	suddenhardware death?
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0611111218530.5646@grond.sourballs.org>
References: <NCEHIMIKBFOBJGNKBMLAAEFECCAA.jimwebb@kc.rr.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.63.0611111218530.5646@grond.sourballs.org>
Message-ID: <45564572.9000708@comcast.net>

David Fleck wrote:
>
> Here's a new question: could simply adding in a stick of memory (256MB 
> PC-3200 400Mhz) fry a power supply? Or is that mere coincidence? This 
> particular memory stick ran fine in my wife's machine for several years.
>
>
>   
I doubt that would fry the power supply directly. But age of the 
hardware is one consideration, nothing lasts forever. Corporate service 
techs are trained extensively on _E_lectro _S_tatic _D_ischarge, and the 
damage it causes. Just touching the case before handling internal 
components is not a guarantee that ESD will not damage something. Often 
the damage is slight, only weakening a tiny trace connection to an IC. I 
have seen electron microscope images of this. What happens is the 
component will fail "mysteriously" a few months down the road.

Mel

I don't have a lot of experience with this, but after reading the man 
pages for fsck and e2fsck I think I would try:
1.  Run e2fsck -ck      This will check for bad blocks.
2. Then e2fsck -pv     This will try to repair automatically with 
verbose output.

From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Sun Nov 12 17:49:15 2006
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Sun, 12 Nov 2006 18:49:15 -0700 (MST)
Subject: [PLUG] superblock problem

Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0611121844090.15328@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>

> Thanks, but I got back the same errors:
>
>
> e2fsck -pv /dev/hdb
> hd2: Attempt to read block from filesystem resulted in short read while 
> reading block 524
> hd2: Attempt to read block from filesystem resulted in short read reading 
> journal superblock
> e2fsck: Attempt to read block from filesystem resulted in short read while 
> checking ext3 journal for hd2

e2fsck is a wrapper for fsck.  If fsck throws these errors, e2fsck
will too.

Here's something that may be worth a try:

http://lists.debian.org/debian-user/2005/03/msg00584.html

It involves dd-ing your hard drive to a big ol' file, and then
mounting the file -o loop.

Carlos Konstanski

From russell at personaltelco.net  Mon Nov 13 08:59:42 2006
From: russell at personaltelco.net (Russell Senior)
Date: 13 Nov 2006 08:59:42 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] cups silliness
In-Reply-To: <86ac3qz3to.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
References: <86ac3qz3to.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
Message-ID: <867ixzi8ox.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>


Russell> I am a cups newbie, so naturally it doesn't take much to make
Russell> me confused.  I have cups running on the various hosts on my
Russell> network, I can print local (to the printer).  I can also
Russell> submit print jobs from the clients, but for some reason the
Russell> remotely-submitted jobs just sit in the printer-local host's
Russell> queue.  Any hints on how to debug this?

Russell>   $ lpq -P dj
Russell>   dj is ready and printing
Russell>   Rank    Owner   Job     File(s)                         Total Size
Russell>   active  russell 235     test.ps                         687104 bytes
Russell>   1st     russell 236     test.ps                         687104 bytes
Russell>   2nd     russell 237     test.ps                         687104 bytes
Russell>   3rd     russell 238     test.ps                         687104 bytes
Russell>   4th     russell 239     test.ps                         687104 bytes

I figured this out last week or so (long enough that I've already
forgotten the fine detail), thought I'd followup with the general
solution.  I may have misled (i don't remember now) on the problem
description slightly.  It is possible that printing locally was not
working afterall.  

Turns out, the host that attached to the printer had its cups
configuration pointing at the printer as a network resource, rather
than as a locally connected usb printer that it was.  As soon as I
fixed that, bingo, everybody started printing.


-- 
Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net

From turner at glsuite.com  Mon Nov 13 09:37:12 2006

> I agree with the cost being low.  But this drive I had is only 3 years
old
> (or even less).  I expect them to last a bit longer than that.


And there lies the problem with the continually downward spiral of
quality vs. the price. People these days seem to only care about how
large of a drive they can get for the money and the only way companies
can offset the cost is lower quality control measures and maybe sub-par
materials. 

Lately it's been performance for me, and getting a "high"(read 10k rpm)
performance drive is well worth the money even if you're only getting an
80 GB drive. 

Because honestly; the bigger storage I have the more crap I
accumulate... which seems to be the same problems with my homes...hmm
insightful.

-mark

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Mon Nov 13 09:16:46 2006
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (Unknown)
Date: Mon, 13 Nov 2006 09:16:46 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] What's the best way to troubleshoot severe and
	suddenhardware death?
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0611111218530.5646@grond.sourballs.org>
References: <NCEHIMIKBFOBJGNKBMLAAEFECCAA.jimwebb@kc.rr.com>
	<Pine.LNX.4.63.0611111218530.5646@grond.sourballs.org>
Message-ID: <1163438207.31129.3.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>

http://www.pcstats.com/articleview.cfm?articleID=864

Is this the board you are troubleshooting?

It looks like a soket processor which is good because
the slot style processors seem to come out easier.
For dead power supply, check to see if the fan is
ceased and if it is I'd strongly suggesting picking
up an Antec unit to replace it.  The other thing to
watch out for is a power supply issue such as 
overloading a UPS or a circuit that would have 
nothing to do with the functioning of your computer.
The first rule of troubleshooting is to make sure
that what you are focusing on is in fact broken.

     --  Michael C. Robinson



From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Mon Nov 13 09:39:03 2006
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (Unknown)

The predecessor was apparently unstable.  This review
says something about needing registered memory if the
board had four slots.  The USB apparently stops 
working on the board reviewed in this article 
beyond 137 megahertz.  Putting memory on this board 
that is faster than what it was designed for probably 
won't work if the reviews I've been running into are 
correct about it being unstable.

Looking at this board, I bet I'd buy it if I didn't
realize it has problems.  The A version of this is
supposed to be okay, but I wonder if MSI really got
the problems fixed.  What is an AMR slot?  It sounds
like a non standard slot that never caught on.

What about an oversized southbridge chipset heatsink
makes it less than robust?  Isn't oversized pretty 
much good and being undersized a problem?

I'd test that I'm troubleshooting the right thing by
plugging in a stable electrical appliance where I'm
taking my power from for this machine.

Question I have if the motherboard isn't all that great,
what is the best replacement for it?

On Mon, Nov 13, 2006 at 05:06:23PM +0000, Vincent Yau wrote:
> I agree with the cost being low.  But this drive I had is only 3 years old 
> (or even less).  I expect them to last a bit longer than that.

Indeed.  And it might still be under warranty.  Some drive manufacturers
have a webpage that lets you punch in a serial number or datecode and
find out if the drive's still under warranty.

If it's not, DO NOT RE-USE IT.  With modern hard drives, once you start
seeing errors, the drive's nearly dead.

I bought a used 60G drive for $30 (before idiscount) at FreeGeek the
other day, BTW.  They test 'em and everything.

need multi-CPU)  will in general run your 32-bit code faster than any

I know the vendors of the codes you're running probably don't want to 
give you benchmark results, but it won't hurt to ask. Meanwhile, given 
that you are dealing with a single-threaded 32-bit app, probably 
floating point intensive, I'd go with whatever has the shallowest 
pipelines and the biggest cache.

Open Source
---------------------
***Getting Started with WSGI 
Python 2.5 added support for the WSGI standard. This is a specification
for web programming that allows interoperability between frameworks and
components. It's also terribly easy to use. Jason Briggs introduces WSGI
and gives the background you need to use it productively.
<http://www.onlamp.com/pub/a/python/2006/11/09/getting-started-with-wsgi.html>


***Open Tools for MySQL Administrators 
The MySQL distribution provides several tools for database developers
and administrators, but they don't always work everywhere. Fortunately,
the worldwide MySQL community has produced plenty of useful tools. Baron
Schwartz surveys the possibilities and offers suggestions for what you
should use.
<http://www.oreillynet.com/pub/a/mysql/2006/10/19/mysql-tools.html>


***Developing High Performance Asynchronous IO Applications 
When concurrency and latency are your bottlenecks, synchronous IO is a
problem--even in a multithreaded or multiprocess model. This is
especially evident when dealing with high volumes of incoming mail,
especially if much of it is spam. Stas Bekman and his team at
MailChannels recently built a scalable, modern, event-based system for
asynchronous IO. Here's how they did it.
<http://www.onlamp.com/pub/a/onlamp/2006/10/12/asynchronous_events.html>

From bwatson1979 at gmail.com  Mon Nov 13 22:33:49 2006
From: bwatson1979 at gmail.com (Benjamin Watson)
Date: Tue, 14 Nov 2006 00:33:49 -0600
Subject: [PLUG] A little more help with Pyramid Linux on Metrix Mark II
Message-ID: <334c4450611132233g5b983a8aq5364ace133c58adc@mail.gmail.com>

All knowing PLUG Listers,

I've posed questions before and have received constructive comments
(which I greatly appreciate), so here goes.

I've got a small wireless mesh network setup in the following topology:

L1<---link1--->R1<---link2--->R2<---link3--->L2

L1 and L2 are laptops (WinXP with wireless cards)

R1 and R2 are mesh routers with dual wireless cards and an ethernet
port (Pyramid Linux).

R1 is connected to the "Internet" via its ethernet port (eth0).

R2 is not connected directly to the Internet (its eth0 port is open).


The routing on R1 and R2 is accomplished by running OSLRd on the
radios on R1 and R2 responsible for maintaining the link2 connection.
For all intents and purposes, OSLRd is merely maintaining routing
tables on R1 and R2.  I use HNA announcements on R1 and R2 to
broadcast connectivity to LANs (wlan0 AP on R1 and R2) and the
Internet (eth0 on R1 only).


Under my current configuration, L1 is hosting a web server and FTP server.
L2 is able to ping L1 over the mesh, and can even access the FTP
server on L1 and transfer files.

Question #1:

However, L2 is unable to pull up the default web page hosted on L1.
I've witnessed a successful TCP handshake between the two, but the
HTTP connection is never established.  What might be blocking this?
IPTABLES on R1 is currently setup with default policies to allow
everything, and performs SNAT to the IP on eth0 to facilitate Internet
access.  IPTABLES on R2 isn't configured at all (defaults to allow
everything).

This same problem occurs when L2 attempts to access the Internet.  L1
can surf the web with no problems (it can even resolve DNS names).
L2, on the other hand, can't resolve DNS names, but even more, it
cannot pull up web sites on the Internet (by IP address) while having
the capability to ping the Internet IP address.

I've installed Lynx on R1 and R2 to troubleshoot the problem and have
verified that R1 can pull up the web page on L1, but R2 cannot (same
symptom as experienced by R2).

Question #2:

I mentioned the DNS issue.  R1 connects to a cable/DSL router via DHCP
and obtains all of the DNS settings "automagically", thereby allowing
L1 to do DNS lookups and resolve names.

Does anyone have a link to a HOWTO or advise for getting DNS
information on R2 via R1, thereby allowing L2 to do DNS lookups and
resolve names?

I've done some poking around regarding dnsmasq and the nameservice
plugin to OSLRd, but the combination of the two leaves me scratching
my head and my attempts to configure this have been uneventful.


Thanks in advance, and thanks for your help in the past!

Ben

> I do a LOT of CAD simulation, mostly with proprietary apps ($$$)

> simple and fast, I do not need fast graphics or fast ethernet or
> sound or big disk or big ram any other wizzy stuff, just the best
> performance on small, single-threaded 32 bit floating-point apps. 
> I will probably be running a RHEL4 clone OS on the machine.
>
> What is the best way to go?  Does an Opteron or a dual-core run
> 32 bit single thread apps faster than a P4 or Atheron?  Given
> fast processor X, which motherboard is most reliable?  Suggested
> websites with reviews?
>
> Keith
>   
I'm not sure spec benchmarks are valid relative to CAD simulation. IIRC 
CAD simulation is floating point intensive and "highly scalar" -- not 
really helped by vector or parallel or concurrent execution. Hence you 
want short clock cycles, large caches and shallow pipelines.

By the way, although you might not be using them, there *are* open 
source CAD codes available, which might make good benchmarks. You could 
compile one that's close in functionality to one you actually want to 
run on your current box and benchmark other machines with it.

It's going to be moderately difficult to find a pure 32-bit non-dual 
core machine these days -- even starter systems have 64-bit dual-core 
chips in them these days. So even if you don't need all that stuff, 
perhaps it's worth getting in the hopes that the application vendors 
will catch up to the hardware in six months or so. :)

-- 
M. Edward (Ed) Borasky, FBG, AB, PTA, PGS, MS, MNLP, NST, ACMC(P)
http://borasky-research.blogspot.com/

From eli.stair at gmail.com  Tue Nov 14 08:18:07 2006
From: eli.stair at gmail.com (Eli Stair)
Date: Tue, 14 Nov 2006 08:18:07 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Linux compute engine
In-Reply-To: <4559CF17.9070003@cesmail.net>
References: <20061113214706.GA3967@gate.kl-ic.com>
	<003e01c707d4$1378e620$8200a8c0@K61800> <4559CF17.9070003@cesmail.net>

Actually, SpecVIEWPERF contains a number of high-end CAD as well as 3D
modeling softwares.  It's the most relevant benchmark available for
comparing hardware for /production/ software, as opposed to games.  I
use this in addition to a number of other codes to regularly
test/compare GL cards & CPU's for use in the 3D effects industry.

/eli

On 11/14/06, M. Edward (Ed) Borasky <znmeb at cesmail.net> wrote:
> Douglas Howard wrote:
> > The SPEC benchmarks and website are one "standard" with
> > published benchmarks on their site.  http://www.spec.org/
> >
> > Look at CPU benchmarks and Results areas of the site.
> > You can also download the benchmark and run it on your candidate system
> > to see how it compares against all the others.
> >
> > Douglas
> > linux at ppcug.org
> >
> > ----- Original Message -----
> > From: "Keith Lofstrom" <keithl at kl-ic.com>
> > To: "PLUG" <plug at lists.pdxlinux.org>
> > Sent: Monday, November 13, 2006 1:47 PM
> > Subject: [PLUG] Linux compute engine
> >
> > I do a LOT of CAD simulation, mostly with proprietary apps ($$$)
> > designed for 32 bit X86.  I want to buy new motherboard; I want
> > simple and fast, I do not need fast graphics or fast ethernet or
> > sound or big disk or big ram any other wizzy stuff, just the best
> > performance on small, single-threaded 32 bit floating-point apps.
> > I will probably be running a RHEL4 clone OS on the machine.
> >
> > What is the best way to go?  Does an Opteron or a dual-core run
> > 32 bit single thread apps faster than a P4 or Atheron?  Given
> > fast processor X, which motherboard is most reliable?  Suggested
> > websites with reviews?
> >
> > Keith
> >
> I'm not sure spec benchmarks are valid relative to CAD simulation. IIRC
> CAD simulation is floating point intensive and "highly scalar" -- not
> really helped by vector or parallel or concurrent execution. Hence you
> want short clock cycles, large caches and shallow pipelines.
>
> By the way, although you might not be using them, there *are* open
> source CAD codes available, which might make good benchmarks. You could
> compile one that's close in functionality to one you actually want to
> run on your current box and benchmark other machines with it.
>
> It's going to be moderately difficult to find a pure 32-bit non-dual
> core machine these days -- even starter systems have 64-bit dual-core
> chips in them these days. So even if you don't need all that stuff,
> perhaps it's worth getting in the hopes that the application vendors
> will catch up to the hardware in six months or so. :)
>
> --
> M. Edward (Ed) Borasky, FBG, AB, PTA, PGS, MS, MNLP, NST, ACMC(P)
> http://borasky-research.blogspot.com/
>

From Jeff.Moore at chemeketa.edu  Tue Nov 14 08:55:35 2006
From: Jeff.Moore at chemeketa.edu (Jeff Moore)
Date: Tue, 14 Nov 2006 08:55:35 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Cant always SSH to box but can ping it for days
In-Reply-To: <20061114042004.71BE747D6@ulke.nakedape.cc>
References: <20061114042004.71BE747D6@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <1163523336.19932.26.camel@sparrow>

Hi all I need some advice. I am fighting a bit of a ghost and don't know
where else to look. I reinstalled my edge box this weekend and have gone
with vmware to containerize all my virtual appliances. Should make
developing and experimenting much easier. Anyway I am having a problem
with the base server responding to tcp traffic. It is sporadic at best
and most of the time it won't respond at all. I can always ping the eth0
interface but cannot ssh to it dependably from my PC. Note that when I
cannot connect I get a "connection refused" error. Me thinks this is
typical of a firewall or acl but there are none in between. The machine
is a fresh install of ubuntu server with openssh, kernel source, a few
libraries, and vmware server installed. On vm1 I have a pfsense server
installed and it is working fine. I can access it via 443 with no
problems and it is passing traffic just dandy. I must be missing
something pretty basic here. Thanks for the help!
(SORRY FOR THE POOR ASCI ART. I AM A VISUAL FELLER) 

       ---------                      --------
 eth1  |       | eth0                 |      |
-------|       |-------(           )--|      |
   |   | ----- |   |   (dell switch)  |  PC  | 
 vmnet1|-|   |-|vmnet2 (           )  |      |
       | |vm1| |                      |      |
       | ----- |                      |      |
       ---------                      --------

Configuration:
-(eth1) is up but is configured with an "ifconfig eth1 0.0.0.0" cmd
I did this because I dont want any traffic to hit eth1 accept for the
traffic destined for the vmnet1(which is bridged to eth1). Probably a
better way to do this... Any tips welcome.. note I put an "up ifconfig
eth1 0.0.0.0" line in my /etc/network/interfaces to do this.
-(vmnet1) is up and has my external ip address. It is bridged via vmware
to eth1
-(eth0) is up and is 10.x.x.10. This is the int having the connection
problems
-(vmnet2) is up and is 10.x.x.1. This is the pfsense gateway. (note that
10.x.x.10 is showing up correctly in the arp tables on this gateway)

Funny things I have noticed:
- Even directly connected(eliminating the dell) to the box it will still
fail. 
- The time to connect with ssh is very slow, I will get a username
prompt and then will wait a good 6 seconds for a password prompt. 
- Also the route command is very slow to respond. It takes forever to
get the default line.
- I have tried to set the ip on eth1 to a private address ie 192.168.x.x
and have also disabled it altogether. The changing of the address had no
effect. The disabling of the interface seemed to help with the stability
of the connection.
- Unplugging the eth1 interface seems to help with stability(ssh is
still slow to authenticate)
- I have also shutdown the vm1 to make sure there were no conflicts and
it didn't change anything.
- I have swapped the configurations of eth0 and eth1 and still have the
same results on different interfaces.

Thanks all! sorry for the long post!
jm


From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Mon Nov 13 11:04:22 2006
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (someone)
Date: Mon, 13 Nov 2006 11:04:22 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Re: local store
In-Reply-To: <1162488760.5260.1.camel@willow.odshp.com>
References: <20061031155414.4EA774740@ulke.nakedape.cc>
	<454977E1.1000609@openboxbuilder.com>
	<1162488760.5260.1.camel@willow.odshp.com>
Message-ID: <1163444663.31129.56.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>

On Thu, 2006-11-02 at 09:32 -0800, Wil Cooley wrote:
> On Wed, 2006-11-01 at 20:45 -0800, Edward Terry wrote:
> > >> Can anyone recommend a Linux-friendly computer store near Vancouver that has 
> > >> good support?
> > > 
> > >    My experiences with ENU on NE 122nd Ave. have consistently been positive.
> > > That's more than I can write about any other retailer in the area.
> > 
> > I called ENU, but they said they don't install or support Linux.
> > 
> > Can anyone recommend a local store that sells computers with Linux 
> > preinstalled and provides good support?  It would be nice if it's near 
> > Vancouver, but anywhere in the metro area will do.
> 
> No, there is no such store, unless you're looking for business-class
> systems.  None of the small, local PC shops have the time to devote to
> learning and supporting Linux and there isn't that much market for it,
> AFAICT.
> 
> Wil

Why Wil?  Are you absolutely certain?  Shops find time to become
certified Microsoft support centers, why not Linux?  What makes you 
say that being a certified Linux support center in a small shoppe 
isn't feasible?  How does incident Linux support work?  Does Redhat
offer the latter?  Has Geek squad ever considered Linux support?  Is
there a greater need for OS-X and Windows support because the source
code is closed, hence a bias in support?  There are plenty of, "I have
not learned...," and, "I don't want to/can't learn...," comments on 
this list despite the openness of most Linux dependent software.  To 
me, that sounds like a potential market.  I think commercial Linux 
support is positive as long as free support benefits from it in 
the long run, the quality is good, and the pricing is reasonably
competitive.  Maybe I don't live in reality on the subject of how
the computer software business works, but if noone knows why it
doesn't work the way many feel it should, how will it ever improve?
If you don't know how or why something is broken, how are you supposed
to fix it?  I don't feel that I have sufficient Linux mastery to start 
a small shoppe providing Linux support, but I bet there are people
on this list with both the skill and the time that could.  So my
question to these individuals is, what's stopping you?  Aside from
Carla's book series, I don't see much being done.

What metric Wil are you using to measure the demand for local Linux
support businesses?  There are Mapinfo groups with apparently paid
members, maybe something similar can contribute to establishing
local Linux support shops.  Buying a Linux compatible hardware
platform that can fill one's needs isn't a task that is diminishing
or going away, especially with OEM's continually attempting to
create proprietary hardware and lock it into closed software 
platforms.  There is more need for small business computer support 
than there is for large business computer support.  Small business 
is the lifeblood of every economy in the world.  Without small
business, there is only a minimal amount of innovation.  What is
the viability of creating a business like Dell that specializes
in a mail order Linux laptop business with lifetime support?  Are
Dell and Gateway the only companies that can do this?  I especially
question the latter notion if Dell and Gateway won't support Linux
as the installed OS.

     --  Michael C. Robinson

Subject: [PLUG] Making 'view as list' the default
In-Reply-To: <4559316E.6030903@bctonline.com>
References: <4557D283.8040807@bctonline.com>	<20061112204849.5a527ec8.johnxj@comcast.net>

In any folder window, select Edit->Preferences.   Under the Views tab, Default 
View, in the 'View _n_ew folders using:'  drop down list, select List View.  After
I did this, all folder I opened are using the list view (except It looks more like
a tree view to me).  I'm assuming Ubuntu here, not Kubuntu.  I haven't found
a way to do this yet outside of a folder window.

- tony

From Jeff.Moore at chemeketa.edu  Wed Nov 15 07:21:31 2006
From: Jeff.Moore at chemeketa.edu (Jeff Moore)
Date: Wed, 15 Nov 2006 07:21:31 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Cant always SSH to box but can ping it for days
References: <20061114200007.C2827478A@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <5B0B1CC91321DE4F947C03D9238B267E020BFF@cccmail2.chemeketa.network>

Think I got it! I noticed this morning that when I pinged the 10.x.x.10 address I would get a .3 ms reply with a TTL=64. However every so often this would change to .7ms reply with a TTL=255 and my connection would drop. Very wierd. Seemed like there was another host with the same address. There were no arp entries in my router to support this theory. Regardless I decided to change the address of the interface. Boom now its been working stable for an hour or so. It responds immediately when I ssh and the ping stays constant at .3ms and TTL=64. I am unable to ping the old address so I am not sure what exactly was going on. Oh and I killed the forward record for the old address. I didnt think ssh was dns dependent. Thanks All!
jm

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Wed Nov 15 09:05:27 2006
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Wed, 15 Nov 2006 09:05:27 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] mutt.octet.filter and application/octet-stream mail
Message-ID: <20061115170527.GB3567@gate.kl-ic.com>


A little annoyance solved ...

Mail attachments of type "application/octet-stream" can be damned
near anything;  M$ mailers expect you to resolve the type by the file
extension.  This makes it easier to propagate viruses, so that everyone
can share.  The /etc/mailcap and .mailcap files don't know what to do
with the extensions.

For those of you using the mutt mail reader, you can use the shell
script at http://www.davep.org/mutt/mutt.octet.filter, which is launched
by "application/octet-stream; mutt.octet.filter %s ; copiousoutput"
in the mailcap file.  I added some code to mutt.octet.filter so it
will launch Acrobat Reader when it sees a pdf (Proprietary! Heretic!
KILL!!!), but for the most part it seems to handle many common types
of octet-stream automagically.

It might be better to do some rewrite rules on incoming mail so that
the octet-stream types are rewritten into "application/xxxxx" for
all the various types, so that mailcap can handle that naturally.
Is there such a tool for postfix?

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

Have you looked at procmail (local mailbox delivery agent for postfix)?

know if there is a built in filter for attachment types, but you should 
be able
to provide that if necessary.

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Tue Nov 14 18:00:29 2006
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (someone)
Date: Tue, 14 Nov 2006 18:00:29 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] iptraf display problems...
Message-ID: <1163556030.870.27.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>

I am specifically noticing that iptraf displays fine on a 2.4.29 
Redhat 7.2 system, but on all my 2.6 kernel based Linux systems,
including Slackware 10.1, it doesn't display right.  It's fine 
inside an x terminal, but not on the console.  Was there some 
change that affected ncurses based programs which I need to be 
aware of between the release of the 2.4 and the 2.6 kernels?

Doing export TERM=vt100 is certainly a workaround, but not a very 
good one.  I tried to google for this problem only to get bogged
down in extraneous hits.

I'm getting ready to pull my last Redhat 7.x box up to 
CentOS 4.3 Final.  Understandably, I don't want to lose 
any functionality that I currently have.

If it's a framebuffer issue, a broken terminal library, etc. 
I'd really like to know.

<gripe>
2.6 was supposed to be a grand reorganization of the kernel after 
the 2.4 series.  It seems to me that: the number of options exploded, 
things got broken, and the documentation to options ratio dropped
sharply.  In a stable kernel, I feel that undocumented options 
should either be documented or removed.  I think the time has come
for the developers of the 2.6 kernel to revisit the documentation
problem and get more aggressive about it.

I know there is tremendous pressure to upgrade in order to be secure.
However, I am not very impressed with the 2.6 kernel yet.  Redhat 9 
and Fedora Core 1 are old systems, though they also appear to be among
the last Linux distributions that are 2.4 kernel based.  I worry that
simplicity that existed when there were fewer kernel options has been
lost as 2.6 has grown.

To help people configure 2.6 properly for what they have verses the
options that exist, has there been any attempt to make a small
distribution on a flash drive that essentially writes a 2.6 config
file based off of hardware detection?  Even if it wouldn't crank 
out an optimal configuration, a stable base configuration is a good 
starting point.
</gripe>

     -- Michael C. Robinson

On Sat, 2006-11-04 at 15:56 -0800, Wil Cooley wrote:
> On Sat, 2006-11-04 at 15:40 -0800, M. Edward (Ed) Borasky wrote:
> 
> > I'd recommend staying away from CentOS. I've used it, and I haven't had
> > any problems whatsoever with it, and it seems to be what it claims to be
> > -- a community-driven rebuild of Red Hat Enterprise Linux. My hesitation
> > to recommend it is based on concerns about the strength of the community
> > relative to the strength of the Fedora community. CentOS is *competing*
> > with Red Hat and Fedora is *co-operating* with Red Hat. That's a *big*
> > difference!
> 
> I disagree.  Regardless of the relationship between CentOS and RHEL,
> CentOS is a very strong choice--it has the stability and familiarity of
> RHEL without the price tag (which honestly isn't that bad at $300/yr).
> If you want to get, say, your RHCE you can learn it on CentOS.
> 
> The additional benefit is that RHEL remains an easy option--if you have
> customers who want to pay for support or want to run a proprietary
> application on a certified platform in production, you can use RHEL and
> use CentOS for dev and QA.
> 
> If you have a customer with a RHEL server which they no longer want to
> pay for support, you can just switch update sources to CentOS and not
> worry about it.
> 
> If you maintain a collection of locally-built packages, you need only
> build them once (per major version).
> 
> Fedora isn't a good choice for commercial uses like this because its
> lifespan is frightfully short and fedora-legacy near death.  I like it
> on my own workstations because I always want the latest there, but my
> servers are all CentOS.
> 
> Wil

I'll second Wil on this so far.  I seem to be moving in this direction
myself.  CentOS is a good system if you want a clean Linux system that
doesn't come with any proprietary software.  It is seems to be a good
choice for stability.  CentOS is not for supporting the latest
and greatest.  The update repository seems very limited.  On the other
hand, upgrades often impact system stability in a negative way.
Theoretically if you don't install experimental compilers etc., you
don't need to update as frequently.

I think all of the major Linux distributions could be pushed 
aside by one that is: documented better, current, and designed to
run in a small footprint.  I wonder what Puppylinux is based on
and if any of the small Linux distributions will compete with 
the RHEL clones?  We are starting to see embedded Linux distributions,
but they seem to be mostly specialty distros that are hard to find out
about.  These distros appear to be very tightly tied to specific
hardware for the most part.  A linux distro that targets certain Linksys
routers which has been mentioned recently on this list comes to mind.

I've tried gentoo and I don't like it.  If you don't like the poor
documentation that comes with the 2.6 kernel, I seriously doubt that
you will like gentoo much.  I'm sorry, but I don't understand portage
very well and I worry that a system that trusts it's package management
system and recompiling software to stay current is going to be nothing
more than a maintenance nightmare.  In a world where no upgrade can
cause loss of functionality or break programs, gentoo might shine.  In
reality, making a mistake in how you compile one package or upgrading
something that can't be without replacing everything is not the sort of
situation you want on a production server.  I think gentoo magnifies the
confusion of updates that people already feel on yum and apt based Linux
systems.  Updating in general in my opinion hasn't been implemented well
in any popular operating system at this time.

     --  Michael C. Robinson

From creswick at gmail.com  Wed Nov 15 12:57:10 2006
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Wed, 15 Nov 2006 12:57:10 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] NTFS Support?
In-Reply-To: <1163581822.870.55.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
References: <20061104010331.66260.qmail@web508.biz.mail.mud.yahoo.com>
	<454BF6B5.50002@schluting.com>
	<1163581822.870.55.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <c834ec80611151257m1981cff6if8d757d9a579022b@mail.gmail.com>

On 11/15/06, someone <plug_0 at robinson-west.com> wrote:
>
> Why can ext2/ext3 filesystems occupy larger partitions than FAT
> filesystems can?  I'm suspicious of NTFS even on Windows 2000/XP.
> I question how NTFS is supposedly an improvement over FAT where
> I'm even more concerned that the tools Microsoft puts out
> developed originally for FAT12/FAT16/FAT32 filesystems aren't
> suited to NTFS.

Why would you expect older tools, which were created for older
technologies to work with newer technologies? You don't need to be a
programmer to realize that an 8-track won't work with a cd player...
this is a similar issue.  AFAIK, FAT filesystems aren't journaled, and
NTFS is -- that alone is enough to make them largely incomparable, and
there are certainly many other minor differences as well.

Keep in mind that those differences are what represent steps *forward*
(in general -- I'm not debating the benefits of FAT over NTFS, or vice
versa, at least not at a file-system tech. level.)  Some advances
simply can't be made while perpetually providing for backwards
compatibility.  Other things just simply aren't worth updating -- I'm
assuming it's far easier to just create a new set of tools for NTFS
than it was to update the old tools to work with all MS filesystems.

> I wish Microsoft was a company that respects
> the GPL.  I'd love to see Microsoft throw out all of it's
> proprietary filesystems in exchange for better OSS replacements.
> For those working on ReactOS, I think installing to an ext3
> instead of an NTFS filesystem is where the focus should be.

I'm not familiar with ReactOS, but if you're targeting Windows users,
it probably helps to support a file system they can read with existing
computers.  Until windows natively supports ext* or reiser or xfs
or... then NTFS/FAT seems a reasonable choice.

> will ever become a complete drop in substitute for XP.  Why
> isn't there a free Windows clone around that's comparable to
> at least Windows 2000 which Microsoft is trying to drop
> support for now?

Because it's really, really, *really* hard to make a clone of
something that big, and it's *extremely* hard to make a clone of
something that big when you have to reverse engineer it, and all it's
bugs, from a black-box perspective.  Surely you're familiar with the
Wine project, and the CrossOver line of products?

--Rogan
