From znmeb at cesmail.net  Wed Nov 15 19:53:38 2006
From: znmeb at cesmail.net (M. Edward (Ed) Borasky)
Date: Wed, 15 Nov 2006 19:53:38 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Linux distro recommendation as a replacement for SuSE
In-Reply-To: <1163584895.870.83.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
References: <200611040914.55603.alex@daniloff.com>	<41704.10.0.1.188.1162670286.squirrel@petelancashire.com>	<200611041247.31287.alex@daniloff.com>
	<454D24ED.4050801@cesmail.net>	<1162684597.7577.25.camel@spartacus.nakedape.priv>
	<1163584895.870.83.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <455BE0C2.1010101@cesmail.net>

someone wrote:
> I think all of the major Linux distributions could be pushed 
> aside by one that is: documented better, current, and designed to
> run in a small footprint.  I wonder what Puppylinux is based on
> and if any of the small Linux distributions will compete with 
> the RHEL clones?
Most of the "small" Linux distros are based on Debian, often via a 
remastered Knoppix LiveCD. Damn Small used to be that way. I personally 
liked Morphix -- they started with Knoppix but made the base LiveCD only 
200 MB and set things up so it was easy to add packages from the Debian 
repositories.

You don't hear a whole heck of a lot about Knoppix these days -- or for 
that matter, many of the other Debian-based distros. And you don't hear 
a whole heck of a lot about Slackware. If you're going down the Debian 
path, you pretty much have to go with Debian itself or with Ubuntu.

If you want a "stable" community distro, Debian stable, CentOS and 
Slackware are probably the most stable. Fedora, being a "proving ground" 
for RHEL, is a lot edgier than those three.

>   We are starting to see embedded Linux distributions,
> but they seem to be mostly specialty distros that are hard to find out
> about.  These distros appear to be very tightly tied to specific
> hardware for the most part.  A linux distro that targets certain Linksys
> routers which has been mentioned recently on this list comes to mind.
>   
Somebody also mentioned Gumstix. I just got one :). Most of the 
ARM-based embedded systems will run off of "buildroot". I don't know 
about how stable it is yet, but my Netstix comes with an Ethernet port 
and a compact flash slot and boots up with DHCP with a functioning ssh 
server and web server. Available software includes Ruby and SQLite, so 
I'm planning to max out the compact flash slot and see if I can get 
Rails running on it. I think *that's* very cool. :)

> I've tried gentoo and I don't like it.  If you don't like the poor
> documentation that comes with the 2.6 kernel, I seriously doubt that
> you will like gentoo much.
Well ... here we disagree. I think Gentoo's overall documentation is the 
best there is! They've led me through some pretty hairy upgrades.
>   I'm sorry, but I don't understand portage
> very well and I worry that a system that trusts it's package management
> system and recompiling software to stay current is going to be nothing
> more than a maintenance nightmare.  In a world where no upgrade can
> cause loss of functionality or break programs, gentoo might shine.  In
> reality, making a mistake in how you compile one package or upgrading
> something that can't be without replacing everything is not the sort of
> situation you want on a production server.
Well ... on a production server, you only need to change something to 
close a security hole or fix a bug in a package you are using. Where I 
think Portage *currently* falls down relative to "up2date/yum" or "apt" 
is in the notification strategy. Up2date, for example, will tell you 
whether an update is a security fix, a bug fix or new functionality. So 
you can choose to only do critical updates. With Gentoo, right now, you 
have to read the change logs. I think I *could* run a production Gentoo 
stable server as easily as someone else could run a CentOS or "Sarge" 
stable server, but I've been working with Gentoo almost exclusively for 
years.

I know the Gentoo people have plans to provide better notification 
strategies, but their user base probably wants other things more, like a 
point and click installer, better LiveCD tools, and more packages in the 
repository.

> I think gentoo magnifies the
> confusion of updates that people already feel on yum and apt based Linux
> systems.  Updating in general in my opinion hasn't been implemented well
> in any popular operating system at this time.
>   
Have a look at http://www.rpath.com/rbuilder/. They talk a good game, 
anyhow.

-- 
M. Edward (Ed) Borasky, FBG, AB, PTA, PGS, MS, MNLP, NST, ACMC(P)
http://borasky-research.blogspot.com/

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Thu Nov 16 07:31:49 2006
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Thu, 16 Nov 2006 07:31:49 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] AMD64 and Linux
Message-ID: <20061116153148.GB17884@gate.kl-ic.com>


I have an AMD Athlon X2 64 dual-core 4600+ machine running.  It
turns out the proprietary CAD tool that I am using has a 64 bit
version I am entitled to, and it will take advantage of dual core.
So a simulation that took 120 seconds on the 32 bit machine now
runs in 18 seconds on both cores.  Since I am running these sims
imbedded in a Perl script, 200 sims in a bunch, that is quite a
time savings.  It is interesting to watch the power meter on the box
jump from 60 watts idle to 120 watts when both cores are cranking.

Why 200 sims?  Checking out many corner cases.  

The software is a proprietary simulator, and has all sorts of copy
control stuff, with an arbitrary built-in 10 second enforced delay
between sims when running dual thread, making the sims 50% slower
than necessary.  I hate "proprietary"[1] software ...

BTW, this is running on top of Scientific Linux 4.4 ("Beryllium")
64 bit SMP, a free RHEL clone from FermiLabs with 3 year upgrade
support.  64 bit is challenging - proprietary [1] apps like Acrobat
Reader look in the wrong places for libraries.  Oh well, it is 
just a compute server.

Keith

[1] "proprietary" is the polite word for "fuck you".

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

> Because it's really, really, *really* hard to make a clone of
> something that big, and it's *extremely* hard to make a clone of
> something that big when you have to reverse engineer it, and all it's
> bugs, from a black-box perspective.  Surely you're familiar with the
> Wine project, and the CrossOver line of products?
> 
> --Rogan

ReactOS is wine turned into an operating system.  There are only two
wine API's apparently that don't work directly in ReactOS, but there
are equivalents to them.

There are only 2 ways I can see to break the Microsoft monopoly, 

A: Don't wait for aggressive marketing of Linux as a desktop
   replacement by the existing players.  Develop and market 
   applications for Linux now.  Linux standards base is your
   friend.

B: The ReactOS project gets further and produces real competition.

Regardless how hard it is to reverse engineer Windows, B is the 
only option until such time that A is taken seriously enough.
I think it's fair to say that noone will effectively replace MS Windows
with a proprietary system because Microsoft will simply buy out that
system via hostile takeover if necessary.  WebTV anyone?  They didn't
last long before Microsoft bought them out.  Open source operating
systems are the only competitor to MS Windows because they are the only
OS'es that Microsoft can't buy out.  Microsoft already owns Apple, so
for those who think Macs are competition to PC's, reconsider.  Until
such time that antitrust means, "you can't buy out every single piece of
proprietary technology within a single market sector," A and B appear to
be what we're stuck with.

Congress in my opinion needs to put some teeth into antitrust and start
enforcing the law, but I doubt that is going to happen anytime soon
judging by the history of antitrust law.  I don't even trust democrats
to push for what needs to be done.  Clinton didn't get the job done
with Microsoft, who is to say that this new Congress will either?

There is a simple way to promote B if you have any money.  Go to
http://www.reactos.org and make a donation.

For those who worry that A is impractical because Linux is changing so
fast.  That issue doesn't seem to stop: Microsoft, Nintendo, Sony, etc.
I see the same Nintendo titles rolled out over and over where the
complexity of programming is on the rise and the Nintendo hardware is
in flux.  Nintendo programmers are definitely updating their programming
skills.  Ditto for Sony with it's playstation.  I hear compiling is
expensive and cloning is hard frequently from Rogan.  Why?  Linus never
said that Linux can't run proprietary applications and even so, there's
the example of IBM which paid for postfix up front and GPL'ed it because
IBM wanted it.  I'm grateful to IBM's investment which gives me a mail
server today, free of charge I might add.  Excuses stink, I can't
believe that a public that deserves choice can't promote it somehow.
OPB supports itself by individual contributions, why not the OSS
community?  If the ReactOS community can attract enough talent and raise
enough money, I think it can go far.  After all, Linux is technically a
black box clone of UNIX.  AT&T locked UNIX System V up if I remember my
history correctly.  If UNIX had remained freely distributable, I doubt
that Linux would have ever been born.

   --  Michael C. Robinson

"Don't buy them then.  But don't bitch at people who want to move into

How much memory is really needed for todays most common applications if
programmers use memory efficiently?  I hear rumors that some
applications are not faster on a dual core system compared to say a
Pentium 4 system.  Pentium 4 systems are 21st century.  What I see is
that dual core systems are very expensive.  I feel they are unproven.
I worry about power consumption considering that the Pentium 4's
of the past already consume too much.  Energy is a major worldwide
problem where every major new computer type that comes out tends to need
more power than it's predecessors.  Since you can't build a safe nuclear
power plant in America due to regulations inspired by a paranoid public
and with our technological capacity to collect solar energy being
woefully inadequate, this problem isn't going to go away anytime soon.  
Other green power options, such as focusing the sun on a water tower
in death valley to run a steam turbine, are dead in the water as well
because of the paranoid public.  Perhaps our energy problems could be
solved today, but the public won't allow this because of it's paranoia.

Moving into the 21st century to me is acquiring computer equipment
that is both modern and 100% Linux compatible.  No 64 bit computers
are yet or there wouldn't be so many postings about software glitches
on them.  I think supporting companies that put out equipment which
Linux has problems with is a mistake.

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Wed Nov 15 20:12:04 2006
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (someone)
Date: Wed, 15 Nov 2006 20:12:04 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Internet growth, RFC 1918 addressing...
Message-ID: <1163650325.9492.68.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>

For private lans with Internet connectivity addressed using an RFC 1918
block, are there any reserved domain names that will never be used on
the Internet?  Are pri, lan, home, ... reserved domain names that people
setting up dns for RFC 1918 networks are allowed to use?  How do you
program bind 9 to realize it shouldn't ask ANY Internet name server
to resolve a particular domain name while keeping this a possibility
for other domain names?  If it is known whether a local name verses 
a remote one is being searched for, there could theoretically be
separate domain systems ( say the global Internet one, and a local
private one ) where the system to use could be indicated at the time 
a program or user queries for a name to be resolved.  This might call
for standard changes to the resolver libraries unfortunately unless 
it can be done another way.  It seems that there really should be a
standard way to query dns nameservers so that one can indicate whether
they are looking for a local name or perhaps a remote name on an
internet/intranet other than the Internet.  Naming whole domains at 
once alone won't be enough I bet as names are exhausted.  Having 
enough physical numbers is only half the problem of addressing 
every Internet connected host.  You can go from 32 billion ip 
addresses to 8 times that or more, but eventually how do you 
come up with enough unique names for all those ips?  As the Internet
grows larger, how long will it be before there is pressure to break
it into multiple smaller networks because it is just too darn big?

Is it standard practice to use one's globally registered domain name
to name hosts connected via RFC 1918 subnets?  Who wants an ip address
without a domain name?  Even if you can't be seen on the Internet, that
doesn't mean you don't want to be seen on your local TCP/IP network.

Using views in bind 9 is an interesting feature, but it feels like a
kludge where I couldn't find any document covering this option on ISC's
web site.

     --  Michael C. Robinson



From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Thu Nov 16 00:25:09 2006
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (someone)
Date: Thu, 16 Nov 2006 00:25:09 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Controversial web site...
Message-ID: <1163665509.9492.153.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>

http://homepages.tesco.net/J.deBoynePollard/FGA/smtp-avoid-helo.html

According to this article, RFC2821 is flawed and anyone who tries to
use Postfix's reject non fully qualified helo hostname feature 
should stop doing so.  The increasing use of NAT is cited as a main 
reason.

I have seen discussions about Microsoft and Netscape mail servers
via google where claims that these servers are highly resistant to
attempts by administrators to set the helo hostname are rampant.  One
would think that the helo hostname for someone emailing out onto the
Internet from behind NAT has to be the hostname of the NAT router, but
it generally isn't and I've always wondered why.  According to this 
web site, no RFC says it has to be.

I'm trying to study smtp-proxying to see about changing my helo after
NAT where this article makes me wonder if that is even common practice,
let alone appropriate.  It appears there are at least some perl based
options, etc. if I should change the helo to match my NAT routers
external address.

My question, why does Postfix's documentation generally recommend
strong checking of the HELO/EHLO string including DNS checks of it?  
If the HELO/EHLO should be resolvable, when will an RFC come out 
mandating that it needs to be resolveable?

I don't know if this is the web site of someone who is frustrated
that has decided to state inaccurately what should be done concerning
helo checks, or if this person is onto something.

One approach is to say don't run a mail server behind NAT.  Security
wise though, who doesn't want to?

If this web site is correct, perhaps a lot of the how to configure
postfix to stop UCE and SPAM information needs to be changed.

How can I soften Postfix 2.0's helo checking features so that
an unknown or supposedly invalid helo leads to an email being
logged, forwarded, or put on hold instead of dropped?

     Michael C. Robinson

offering that Linux works perfectly on where word of mouth
can convince people to favor it.  This will effectively hurt 
AMD if the company won't get serious enough about Linux
and either put something better out or release OSS drivers 
for the problematic integrated devices.

From heinlein at madboa.com  Thu Nov 16 09:57:51 2006
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Thu, 16 Nov 2006 09:57:51 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Internet growth, RFC 1918 addressing...
In-Reply-To: <1163650325.9492.68.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
References: <1163650325.9492.68.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.OSX.4.64.0611160948330.19126@arrowhead.galois.com>

On Wed, 15 Nov 2006, someone wrote:

> How do you program bind 9 to realize it shouldn't ask ANY Internet 
> name server to resolve a particular domain name while keeping this a 
> possibility for other domain names?

Just declare your local instance of BIND to be the master for the 
namespace in question. Your named.conf will look something like

  // where to find info on zones for which we're not master
  // or secondary
  zone "." IN {
    type hint;
    file "named.root";
  }

  // declare ourselves masters of the .robinson DNS space,
  // making hostnames like www.robinson or ftp.robinson
  // perfectly legal for all hosts that use this DNS server
  // for name resolution
  zone "robinson" IN {
    type master;
    file "zone.robinson";
  }

And so forth. I did a BIND writeup for Carla Schroder a couple years 
ago that has some practical examples and some links to weightier 
reference works:

  http://www.madboa.com/geek/soho-bind/

> Is it standard practice to use one's globally registered domain name 
> to name hosts connected via RFC 1918 subnets?

Practices vary widely. Often, internal subnets will be apportioned a 
distinctive slice of the public namespace like "intranet.bigcorp.com," 
but that's certainly not the only way to do it. If you control both 
the .net and .com versions of your namespace, you could reserve .net 
for internal and .com for external addresses...

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

> Having struggled for nearly a month trying to get a laptop with ATI
> chipset to work properly with Linux and finally returning it for one
> with NVidia, I say "NVidia or nuthin.'"

I'd look for an Intel-integrated video if I were you.  I was impressed
by the fact that with X.org 7.1, my work machine with the "crappy" Intel
video just worked with 3D-acceleration, whereas my home machine with a
"hot" NVidia card is a constant headache, dealing with binary drivers
and whatnot.  I wish everyone else would get on the "open-source driver
for our 3D chipsets" bandwagon.

Wil

Nonsense. AS far as I can search MS bought 150,000 non-voting series A
convertible preferred shares in 1997 for $150 million. They have since
then sold them according to e-trade and a 2003 SEC filing from Apple.

They have owned Apple stock BUT NO WHERE NEAR 49%. Please don't pull
numbers out of your ass.
 
> Why did Apple dump Motorola?  I hear that Motorola
> processors have tended to lag behind Intel's, but I've also
> heard that they tend to be better engineered than Intel's
> offerings as Motorola catches up.

The PowerPC line of processors were JOINTLY developed my IBM and
Motorola with input from Apple. IMHO I believe the switch from a RISC
based Processor to x86 chips by apple was a matter of price and speed.
Not shoddy engineering. 

> If it's rubbish that Microsoft owns Apple, great.  I
> seriously doubt that it is though with Apple going to
> Intel effectively putting to death a significant
> source of competition to the WinTel monopoly.

How does architecture change affect Apples ability to compete with
Microsoft? If anything, switching to cheaper, EASIER to replace hardware
will be a major contributing factor to Apples continued success.  As of
October 18, 2006(according to Gartner) apple has 6.1 percent of the US
PC market. That is the ONLY PC maker that does not bundle Microsoft's
OS. They are right behind gateway, hp and dell respectively to hold the
4th position. 
That is a Small percentage but it is FAR greater than any Linux desktop
sales. 
 
> WebTV isn't separate from Microsoft even though it
> started out that way.  Why not Apple?

WebTV was a move by Microsoft to try to capture what they saw as an
emerging market. There is something alluring about being able to just
turn the TV on, check you email and then flip to Histories Mysteries.
What Microsoft didn't count on was dramatic PC price decreases and
continually dropping ISP charges.
I can even understand why your talking about WebTV in the same
conversation as a Microsoft acquisition of Apple.

> If Microsoft doesn't own Apple, why does Apple even bother
> with IE and Outlook in Mac OS-X 10.3 and later?

Umm.... Apple doesn't develop IE for the Mac, Microsoft does. Just like
Microsoft releases versions of Office on the Mac. Office is Microsoft's
cash cow, if Linux had 6.4% of the desktop market I bet you $50 that we
would see office released for it. Plus IE for the Mac has not been
developed since June of 2003, its stuck at Version 5 and there is no
real reason for Mac users to use it with browsers like Safari and
Firefox available. 

As far as Outlook goes, as far as I can research there is a version of
Outlook for the Mac called "Outlook 2001" that was released for Mac OS9
for the sole purpose of Mac clients to be able to access exchange
servers. OSX has a program called Entourage for interfacing with
exchange. 
 
But both of these are DEVELOPED by Microsoft for the Mac and not
Visa-versa.

I don't own a Mac, nor have I ever owned one, but I don't like people
saying absolutely random things just to spur potential arguments over
the internet. Especially when the random statements make absolutely no
sense.

From rsteff at comcast.net  Fri Nov 17 12:18:22 2006
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Fri, 17 Nov 2006 13:18:22 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Printer choice
In-Reply-To: <455E0D99.3060505@dslnorthwest.net>
References: <455E0D99.3060505@dslnorthwest.net>
Message-ID: <455E190E.4050009@comcast.net>

Denis Heidtmann wrote:
> It looks like I am in the market for a color printer. I will be
> replacing an HP Deskjet 930C. It is home use, low volume.  Some
> pictures; mostly text. I am running Kubuntu dapper.

Is your "mostly text" black on white? If the quantity of color work is
low, you might consider an inexpensive laser printer for home, and use
one of the many office services houses (Office Depot, Kinkos/FedEx, etc)
for your color work. Take them a .pdf and they can give you what you
expect the image to look like.

The last printer I bought (this past summer) was an Epson Photo R220.
The warning from the guys at The Printer Place (on SE Powell near 17th)
was to be sure to print the test page once every week or two so the
color heads don't clog. Look for a way to be sure you are only printing
black in the driver (I haven't tried this on my Linux machine so I don't
know what CUPS offers in the way of controlling what colors are used
when printing black). I didn't discover this until I noticed that the
color cartridges were depleting themselves, even though I rarely printed
anything with color.

Other than that, I've been happy with the R220. Now that the color ink
is almost gone, I've switched back to an old Brother HL-730DX laser
printer. Drums are only available from a remanufacturer (about $50), but
the toner is a separate replacement item that is still available, and
the drum will last through several toner cartridges.

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens

I thought Alan might be a bit busy this week.  He told me I might be on 
my own to start the meeting on Monday, so I guess this is self-serve 
month :-)  Here's a bit more info.

Topic:

  WxPerl:  What I Learned the Hard Way
    An architectural overview of using the wxWidgets C++ GUI toolkit
    with Perl on Linux, Mac, and Windows.

OR:  How to have a decent and perlish architecture in spite of being 
subjected to obscenely verbose reams of C++ documentation, overly 
academic examples, slow wikis, and infectious misconceptions.

  PLUG Bonus:  tricks for developing Windows software on Linux

  o wxWidgets -- what it is and what it can do for you
  o interlude:  mac users heckling loudly
  o Wx (aka wxPerl) -- crazy, but true!
  o dotReader:  an open-source e-book reader written in Perl
  o getting started / documentation
  o cross platform development (aka fun with vnc)
  o installation (aka fun with make/nmake/bake/shake/quake)
  o getting past getting started
  o code generation (or not)
  o event driven OOP -- how to have your cake without eating your hair
  o callbacks with closures, methods, and accessors
  o testing
  o building
  o testing
  o packaging and deployment on windows, mac, and linux
  o no really, testing

I've spent the last several months designing and building a large 
(10kLOC and growing in 81 modules) e-book reader system complete with a 
comprehensive test suite, pluggable, reskinnable architecture, 
cooperative multitasking, network-transparent connectivity, blah, blah, 
blah.  The dotReader source code will be released as GPL on Nov. 22nd.

This may even include a live demo.

Mark A. Turner wrote:
>> Why did Apple dump Motorola?  I hear that Motorola
>> processors have tended to lag behind Intel's, but I've also
>> heard that they tend to be better engineered than Intel's
>> offerings as Motorola catches up.
> 
> The PowerPC line of processors were JOINTLY developed my IBM and
> Motorola with input from Apple. IMHO I believe the switch from a RISC
> based Processor to x86 chips by apple was a matter of price and speed.
> Not shoddy engineering.

 From what I've gathered it would have been impossible to compete with thermals and 
power consumption vs cpu power with ppc chipsets as compared vs. x86, so that would 
suggest a purely non-speed related choice. Apple really focuses on the laptops, the 
desktops are IMO marginal business for them and likely far less profitable.

As for price, one could argument that AMD might have beaten Intel, but one should assume 
that both Intel and AMD have shown designs way before anyone else in the market when 
Apple started shopping, and at the time Intel likely had quite an edge with Yonah/Intel 
Core.

On top of that IBM is taking power in a direction which seems orthogonal to 
desktop/office design. It all just seems too logical to me.

Cheers,

Auke


From johnxj at comcast.net  Fri Nov 17 19:25:13 2006
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Fri, 17 Nov 2006 19:25:13 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Formatting thumb drive
Message-ID: <20061117192513.013ee621.johnxj@comcast.net>

About a month ago I lost my 512 MB thumb drive. While waiting for it to
show up in the bottom of the washing machine (where such devices always
end up), I decided I needed a thumb drive badly enough that I bought a
brand new 1 GB drive. Last week the first one appeared in the bottom of
my backpack, so now I have two thumb drives. 

Both are SanDisk Cruzer drives. The first one performs just fine. The
second one also performs fine, but when I stick it in a USB port at a
PSU lab computer, Windows XP finds an auto-launch file that pops up a
SanDisk browser utility. This utility doesn't launch on Linux,
presumably because it is just a Windows utillity. Now, the browser
utility is really annoying. It has SanDisk plastered all over it and
stuff. There is an .exe file on the drive that evidently launches the
utility. I tried to nuke the .exe file while at the university, but
Windows XP refused to delete it. Later, with the drive on my Linux
computer I successfully deleted it.

I thought that would take care of the matter, but the next day I put it
in the USB port on a PSU computer. The damn utility popped back up
again. Lo and behold, the .exe file was back! So, sitting at the PSU
computer I pulled my Ubuntu laptop out, booted it, and stuck the thumb
drive in its USB port. The .exe file was there again. So I nuked it
again. Then I put the thumb drive back in the PSU computer. The utility
popped back up and the .exe file was back on the thumb drive!

Now, this has me seriously pissed off. If SanDisk wants to blast me
with their advertising, then they should pay *me* to use their drive.
My questions for this group are: HTF did SanDisk do this? Has SanDisk
made a pact with the devil Gates? And do I assume correctly that if I
use my Linux computer to reformat the drive (FAT32), I will finally
exorcise the evil installed on it by SanDisk?

>   
Well ... have you tried formatting it on the Windows machine? My 
experience has been that formatting a USB drive on Linux

mkfs -t vfat -F 32 /dev/sdxn

gives you something that Linux has no problems with, but Windows may or 
may not like. However, if you format it on Windows, Linux won't have a 
problem with it.

Now -- on to mounting. You might need to mount your flash drive 
"umask=000" on Linux. In some cases, you'll need to be root to write on 
it if you don't do that.

Finally, unless you want to move stuff around between Windows, MacOS and 
Linux systems on it, there's really not much reason to format it FAT32. 
If you're going to use it exclusively on Linux, ext2 is a much better 
way. For one thing, the bogosities associated with upper and lower case 
translations go away, and the umask=000 isn't necessary.

-- 
M. Edward (Ed) Borasky, FBG, AB, PTA, PGS, MS, MNLP, NST, ACMC(P)
http://borasky-research.blogspot.com/

Maybe, but why do you need to?  (Formatting a usb drive on Linux may 
render it incompatible with some computers (I've seen trouble just 
trying to mount it on different linux boxen), though that seems to have 
been healed in recent kernels.)

I'm wondering if you're actually getting it deleted or just wandering 
into something funky with the automounter.  If you do the following, do 
you still see the files on the second ls?  If not, then try the windows 
box and see if something really funky is going on.

  mount /dev/sda1 /media/usb
  ls -a /media/usb/
  # rm whatever files offend you
  umount /media/usb
  mount /dev/sda1 /media/usb
  ls -a /media/usb/

--Eric

plain USB flash drive.

evidently they all now come with this craptacular U3 junkware.

Gennies are fine. We tested ours weekly and it worked great.
>
> how many servers are you talking about?
We are looking for hosting for 6 1U servers:

  2 Front-end request servers
  3 Mongrel proxy request recipient servers to handle Ruby requests
  1 MySQL server
> finding 24x7x365 on site remote hands is expensive, and the best 
> you'll get is someone on call for ~100/hour.  at that point it gives 
> you a warm body to hit the keyboard - expect more for someone who 
> knows what to do if your system reports any error message.  i'd 
> suggest any of the equnix facilities, but they are all out of state.
I'm comfortable with out-of-state servers. It's natural, of course, that 
I look to servers in Oregon first. We host servers that automatically 
process condominium transactions. Paying the invoice for ISP staff 
on-site is a lot less painful than explaining to your client why their 
site isn't online processing transactions. I haven't been there 
personally but it's pretty clear to me when you ask that it's a lot 
easier to ask a higher price for critical service up front than to try 
to reclaim a customer's trust after a system failure.
>
> colo is usually billed at 95%, or a CIR.  fibre loop bandwith could be 
> anywhere from a 512k/commit  to 1gb/commit.   buying that much transit 
> capacity is expensive unless you've got the capital.
While we're always looking to keep costs to a minimum, we never do so at 
the expense of top service.

Thank you for your recommendation--it's spot-on to what I need.

ISP now. It'll be online, (meekly) I promise."

That's a conversation you _do_not_ want to have. EasyStreet has a plan 
available for just this arrangement.

From carla at bratgrrl.com  Mon Nov 20 16:41:49 2006

On Monday 20 November 2006 13:09, Ed Sawicki wrote:
> Carla Schroder wrote:
> > On Monday 20 November 2006 12:00, Ed Sawicki wrote:
> >> I'm running Fedora Core 5 with Gnome. When I click on
> >> Help|Contents for any graphical application, nothing
> >> appears. I'm on a deadline so I don't have the time now
> >> to search for answers. I'm hoping someone here can give
> >> me a quick answer.
> >>
> >> Thanks,
> >>
> >> Ed
> >
> > My guess is the help docs didn't get installed. Usually they're separate
> > packages, so yum search [packagename] ought to find them.
>
> This resulted in:
>
> ...
> Setting up repositories
> core
> Cannot find a valid baseurl for repo: core
> Error: Cannot find a valid baseurl for repo:core
>
>
> Now the Gnome Applications | Add/Remove Software
> is also broken.

Most likely this means the servers in your /etc/yum.repos.d/ are down, or your 
config files are goobered. You should have a fedora.repo file that looks 
something like this:

[base-i386]
name=base-i386
baseurl=http://download.fedora.redhat.com/pub/fedora/linux/core/5/i386/os/
gpgcheck=1
enabled=1

Every repository needs a separate configuration file. 

This is a good page on configuring Yum repositories:
http://www.physics.wustl.edu/~alford/linux/yum_summary.html

From heinlein at madboa.com  Tue Nov 21 08:43:58 2006
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)

	<8e97c2a60611200013k12506e6doc0233c73ed95912c@mail.gmail.com>
	<20061121130110.GA7362@patch.com> <456323CE.1050501@spek.org>
Message-ID: <Pine.OSX.4.64.0611210831140.21002@arrowhead.galois.com>

On Tue, 21 Nov 2006, Brent Rieck wrote:

> Michael Rasmussen wrote:
> > It is work?
> > 
> > Aside from periodic tweaking of the spam filtering engines, 
> > where's the work?
> 
> sounds like you have a business opportunity for those that don't 
> want to deal with their own mail server, no matter how little work 
> it may be.

Once an e-mail system is up and running (not an easy task, but it's 
essentially a one-time thing), the time-consuming work is two-fold:

  * user and list maintenance: accounts, aliases, and lists
  * customization support: filters, shared folders, etc

That sort of work may be carried out by a systems staff, but it's 
defined and driven by the business customer. Unless that's customer's 
needs are *very* static, my opinion is that it's impossible for a 
viable business to not "want to deal with their own mail server."

You may not think of accounts, aliases, et al. as the real work of 
maintaining a mail infrastructure, but imho it is. It's possible to 
outsource the systems end of the deal, but the day-to-day tasks never 
go away because they're business issues, not technical ones.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

On Tue, 21 Nov 2006, M. Edward (Ed) Borasky wrote:

> Rich, have I got an editor for you!! It's called Leo (Literate Editor for 
> Outlines). It's written in Python and does exactly that sort of thing. If 
> you're spoiled by vim or emacs, it will connect to both of them.

Ed,

   It looks like a stand-alone product with tcl/tk for a GUI widget set. I'll
look to see if its functionality can be used from within emacs.

   The reason I'm on this issue is that some of my module files are getting
large, and I want to navigate quickly among bound methods and other
functions. Years ago (mid-1980s to mid-1990s), when I was writing in C on
DOS I used a wonderful editor called Multi-Edit. It had the ability to
collapse the code based on a starting column. So, for example, since all
functions began in column 1, I could collapse the code and see only the
first line of each function. Moving the cursor to that line and expanding
the display put me just where I wanted to be. Much as I like emacs, I would
like to regain that capability.

   Speedbars (thanks, Ali!), and other options may do the trick. With
speedbars active I can see all the files in the directory, but I need to see
if I can tag classes and methods in .py files so they're directly
accessible, too.

Thanks,

Rich

Ed,

   I've discovered two wonderfully useful things this morning: 1) speedbars
is installed in my emacs ('m-x speedbars' brings it right up), and B)
clicking with both buttons on a .py file name icon displays the included
classes while a double click on the class name icon displays the included
methods. This is exactly the functionality I sought (thank you again, Ali!)
and even better than Multi-Edits approach because it's in a separate window
and lets me work in the main display while it's open.

I am curious whether you know about ECB, or Emacs Code Browser.  It opens
speedbar and a few other neat buffers when you do an "M-x ecb-activate". 
One of the buffers is a class browser that I have seen work well for java,
C, lisp and php.  I'll bet it can do python too.  The buffer shows info
like the following (a java example).  If you middle-click a line, it jumps
you to that part of the code.

[+] Imports
[-] +FdkFileUtils : class
   [+] Parents
   [-] Variables
      -fileManager : FileManager
   +FdkFileUtils (username : String,password : String,serverUrl :
String,session : HttpSession)
   +FdkFileUtils (session : HttpSession)
   +addCategoryToDocument (documentItem : Item,categoryDefinition :
NamedValue[]) : Item
   +createFolder (name : String,description : String,parentId : long) : Item
   +createProjectFolder (name : String,description : String,library :
String) : Item
   +getFileManager () : FileManager
   +getFolderAttributes () : AttributeRequest[]
   +getLibrary (library : String) : Item
   +getLibrary (library : String,attributeRequest : AttributeRequest[]) :
Item
   +getProjectTemplate () : Item
   +resolvePath (path : String) : Item
   +resolvePath (path : String,attributeRequest : AttributeRequest[]) : Item
   +updateFolder (folderId : long,attributes :
NamedValue[],attributeRequest : AttributeRequest[]) : Item
   +uploadDocument (document : Document) : Item
   -createHttpConnection (url : URL) : HTTPConnection
   -getPathFromItem (item : Item) : String
   -getRequestedAttributeFromItem (item : Item,attributeName : String) :
Object
   -initializeFileManager () : void
   -setFileManager (fileManager : FileManager) : void
[+] Package

> 
> Most likely this means the servers in your /etc/yum.repos.d/ are down, or your 
> config files are goobered. You should have a fedora.repo file that looks 
> something like this:
> 
> [base-i386]
> name=base-i386
> baseurl=http://download.fedora.redhat.com/pub/fedora/linux/core/5/i386/os/
> gpgcheck=1
> enabled=1
> 
> Every repository needs a separate configuration file. 
> 
> This is a good page on configuring Yum repositories:
> http://www.physics.wustl.edu/~alford/linux/yum_summary.html
>

for ANY graphical program. I guess it's time to reinstall -

On 11/21/06, Michael Rasmussen <mikeraz at patch.com> wrote:
> Chris Williams wrote:
> > Hosting your own email is more work than it seems to be worth.
>
> It is work?
>
> Aside from periodic tweaking of the spam filtering engines, where's the work?
>
Sometimes my email server(s) work trouble free and everything is fine.
This will go on for months, but then something will go wrong.
I mean, I always seem to have some unexpected difficulty come up every
now and then.

I have had email servers compromised or attacked with DOS attacks
several times - maybe as often as once every two years.

I'm always fighting SPAM or trying to break thru SPAM filters.
One of my domains was miss handled in the past (before it was mine)
and is now SPAM MAGNET CENTRAL.
I need very good SPAM filtering for this domain.
Google does a fair job, but even google has problems with accounts on
this domain.

At times, I have had an isp change my ip number, which would be fine -
except the new ip number was in everyone's black hole list.  Ever try
to get an ip number removed from a black hole list?  Some of the black
hole list administrators won't remove an ip for any reason.  Or have
you ever tryed to send mail to someone whose mail server is
mistakingly blocking your domain?  Or have you ever set your SPAM
filters too tight and blocked out important mail?

Believe me, problems happen with mail servers.

David Mandel

On Mon, 2006-11-20 at 08:54 -0800, Larry Brigman wrote:
> Hello Pluggers,
> 
> I need some help understanding how to do something to an
> older kernel.
> I need to backport the kexec feature to a 2.6.10 kernel to
> make use of kdump for kernel core dumps.
> My current attempts have proved, I need to learn more.
> 
> Please lets limit the discussion to how to accomplish the task
> at hand or an alternate method to capture kernel panics with  a core

>

and ships with kexec/kdump; there's probably a patch specifically for
it.  Porting it forward from 2.6.9 to 2.6.10 is probably much easier
than porting 2.6.18 back to 2.6.10.

From chaz at bctonline.com  Tue Nov 21 22:16:18 2006
From: chaz at bctonline.com (Charles Sliger)
Date: Tue, 21 Nov 2006 22:16:18 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] vim: brown spaces
Message-ID: <B0026973648@bct-mail.bctonline.com>

When I edit a file using vim, the space characters are displayed as brown
blocks.

Is there a way to tell vim to display the space character as whitspace?

 

Charles L. Sliger,  Information Systems Engineer,  chaz at bctonline.com

"No matter where you go, there you are..."

 

 



From pm at nellump.net  Tue Nov 21 22:20:47 2006
From: pm at nellump.net (Paul Mullen)
Date: Tue, 21 Nov 2006 22:20:47 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] vim: brown spaces
In-Reply-To: <B0026973648@bct-mail.bctonline.com>
References: <B0026973648@bct-mail.bctonline.com>
Message-ID: <20061122062047.GA3939@nellump.ath.cx>

On Tue, Nov 21, 2006 at 10:16:18PM -0800, Charles Sliger wrote:
> When I edit a file using vim, the space characters are displayed as
> brown blocks.
> 
> Is there a way to tell vim to display the space character as
> whitspace?

If you have search pattern highlighting enabled, and your last search
was for whitespace (unusual, but it happens), then that could be the
cause.  Run a new search for something highly unlikely (e.g.,
"asdfasdf"), or disable search highlighting (":nohlsearch").

-- 
Paul


From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Tue Nov 21 22:41:10 2006
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Tue, 21 Nov 2006 23:41:10 -0700 (MST)
Subject: [PLUG] vim: brown spaces

> When I edit a file using vim, the space characters are displayed as brown
> blocks.
>
> Is there a way to tell vim to display the space character as whitspace?
>
>
>
> Charles L. Sliger,  Information Systems Engineer,  chaz at bctonline.com
>
> "No matter where you go, there you are..."

Prehaps you might want to sample the various coloschemes in vim.  Try
":colorscheme <TAB> <TAB> <TAB> etc." to cycle through all the
available ones.  If none of these work for you, it is possible to make
your own.

I like colorscheme "ron", personally.  Highlighted text is red in this
colorscheme, I believe.

Carlos Konstanski

From johnxj at comcast.net  Wed Nov 22 10:53:16 2006
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Wed, 22 Nov 2006 10:53:16 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Web site programming
Message-ID: <20061122105316.0fd4f524.johnxj@comcast.net>

Consider this a question from a total dumbass who knows zero about
website creation.

I want to create a website that will function as an electronic version
of flash cards. There would be a list of questions, each with a
drop-down selection tool. When the student has finished selecting the
answers for the page, there will be a button for "check answers."
Clicking on the button would then present the student with the
drop-downs showing the correct answer, and if the student selected the
wrong answer, it would show the correct answer in red. Perhaps a little
score box with a percentage correct would be nice as well.

There would be several hundred questions. Each time the student opened
the page it would present 20-30 or so selected at random from the
several hundred. Thus, the student would always be presented with a new
set of questions. The student could use the website to practice until
they finally get an acceptable score.

There is an additional problem to surmount: The drop-downs and some of
the text on the page must be displayed in a font that has a complete
set of characters for the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA). There
are several available that are open source and freely distributable
(Junicode comes to mind). The problem I have discovered is that many
peoples' browsers do not display the characters properly. I have never
had a problem on my Linux computer, but I think it is because Firefox
and Opera both installed themselves with UTF-8 in the settings by
default. Or maybe it's because Ubuntu set UTF-8 as the default for the
whole operating system. I just know it works and I never had to mess
with anything to get it to work. I note at PSU computer labs the
browsers are set to Western-something, and they do not always display
the characters properly. Whatever it takes to get the students'
browsers to display the characters properly must be added as an
instruction page. That means that I need to figure out what settings
need to be fixed for each popular browser on each popular OS.

The first set of problems would contain questions like:

1. voiced alveolar stop        [ ]  <-- drop-down with IPA characters
2. voiceless velar fricative   [ ]  etc.

Other than needing the display the IPA characters correctly, I'm pretty
sure this is just a matter of tying a database with fields for the
"question" and for the IPA characters to the page. I have no idea how
this is done, but I assume I can learn it. However, I also want to make
a similar page where the "question" is a sound file, using the same IPA
character drop-downs as above. This may be a bigger problem. I know it
is possible to have sounds on a website -- there are lots of them out
there that I have been using. However, would it be possible to tie
several hundred sound files in a database for the questions, so that
the sound files would be presented randomly? In other words, in the
example above, instead of "voiced alveolar stop" and "voiceless velar
fricative" I want icons for sound files to be displayed. The student
would click on the icon, the sound clip would play in whatever plugin
they have installed in their browser, then the student would select the
correct IPA character from the drop-down. When finished the student
would click on the "check answers" button, etc.

I'm assuming that I have a long learning curve ahead of me to figure
out how to do all of this. But before I decide to spend winter break
learning website creation with databases, is the above possible? And
does anyone have hints for how to get browsers to display the
characters correctly?

From aaron at bavariati.org  Wed Nov 22 12:03:53 2006
From: aaron at bavariati.org (Aaron Burt)
Date: Wed, 22 Nov 2006 12:03:53 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Web site programming
In-Reply-To: <20061122105316.0fd4f524.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20061122105316.0fd4f524.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <20061122200353.GA8121@syrinxpc.com>

On Wed, Nov 22, 2006 at 10:53:16AM -0800, John Jason Jordan wrote:
> Consider this a question from a total dumbass who knows zero about
> website creation.
> 
> I want to create a website that will function as an electronic version
> of flash cards. There would be a list of questions, each with a
> drop-down selection tool. <snip>

Cute.  More a multiple-choice short test, rather than flashcards?
Would there be time limits or set response times?

> There would be several hundred questions. Each time the student opened
> the page it would present 20-30 or so selected at random from the
> several hundred. Thus, the student would always be presented with a new
> set of questions. 

Would this be plain ol' random, where there could be repeats, or a
shuffle (like a deck of cards), where there would be no repeats?

> There is an additional problem to surmount: The drop-downs and some of
> the text on the page must be displayed in a font that has a complete
> set of characters for the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA).

IMO the best way to deal with this is to simply use GIF images of the
IPA characters.  Make sure the images are pulled from static URLs on
the webserver so the browser can cache them easily.

You do lose the ability to have the characters in drop-down boxes, but
for a multiple-choice test, it's nicer to have all the options lined-up,
visible and clickable rather than hidden inside a combobox.

It's also possible to make the GIF/IPA thing configurable; maybe there's
even a reliable way to ask the browser if it can display IPA.

> Other than needing the display the IPA characters correctly, I'm pretty
> sure this is just a matter of tying a database with fields for the
> "question" and for the IPA characters to the page. I have no idea how
> this is done, but I assume I can learn it.

Most definitely.  Any way you like from Perl/CGI to Ruby on Rails.

> However, I also want to make a similar page where the "question" is a
> sound file, using the same IPA character drop-downs as above. This may
> be a bigger problem. I know it is possible to have sounds on a website
> -- there are lots of them out there that I have been using. However,
> would it be possible to tie several hundred sound files in a database
> for the questions, so that the sound files would be presented
> randomly?

Yup.  Databases can hold anything, including pathnames to the
soundfiles, or the soundfiles themselves.  Might want to have a
configuration thingy at the beginning to choose what sort of soundfiles
to use.  (I.e. "Click here.  If that doesn't work, click here.")

Ever since I made an ugly voicemail-retrieval webpage for an Asterisk
server, I've been looking for good examples of pages with clickable
cross-platform sounds.  If you run across any, lemme know.

On Wed, 22 Nov 2006 12:03:53 -0800
Aaron Burt <aaron at bavariati.org> dijo:

> > I want to create a website that will function as an electronic version
> > of flash cards. There would be a list of questions, each with a
> > drop-down selection tool. <snip>
> 
> Cute.  More a multiple-choice short test, rather than flashcards?
> Would there be time limits or set response times?

No time limits or set response times. It would be for practice sessions
that students could do on their own time. A learning resource, not a
required activity. It would be best if it could be made "fun," that is,
perhaps a reward if the score on a page is high enough or something. 

> > There would be several hundred questions. Each time the student opened
> > the page it would present 20-30 or so selected at random from the
> > several hundred. Thus, the student would always be presented with a new
> > set of questions. 
> 
> Would this be plain ol' random, where there could be repeats, or a
> shuffle (like a deck of cards), where there would be no repeats?

There should be repeats. That would increase the difficulty level, but
that is a good thing. Students do not learn the answers if they derived
the right answer by counting cards. (I hate Bridge, because I can never
remember what has been played.) 

> > There is an additional problem to surmount: The drop-downs and some of
> > the text on the page must be displayed in a font that has a complete
> > set of characters for the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA).
> 
> IMO the best way to deal with this is to simply use GIF images of the
> IPA characters.  Make sure the images are pulled from static URLs on
> the webserver so the browser can cache them easily.
> 
> You do lose the ability to have the characters in drop-down boxes, but
> for a multiple-choice test, it's nicer to have all the options lined-up,
> visible and clickable rather than hidden inside a combobox.

OK, this might work. But there are 110 IPA "letters," plus about 20
superscripts, and about 30 diacritics, any of which could appear on
about half the characters. Suppose I put GIFs of all ~160 characters at
the top of the page, and each question just has a box in which to type
the character, two spaces wide. The student puts the cursor in the box
next to a question, clicks on the appropriate GIF image(s), and the
character with diacritics and/or superscripts appear in the box. This
would be better than a drop-down box because, as you point out, all the
glyphs are visible at once. The only drawback is the space on the page
that the display of characters takes up.

> It's also possible to make the GIF/IPA thing configurable; maybe there's
> even a reliable way to ask the browser if it can display IPA.

If I am correct and the secret is to set the browser to UTF-8, then
that's what the site needs to query the browser about. However, the IPA
font must appear in the box next to the question when the student
clicks on a GIF image, and that means the box must be set to use a font
that has the IPA characters. Windows boxes have Arial Unicode and
Lucida Sans Unicode, both of which have the full IPA character set. I
assume there is a way to set the boxes to use a certain font.

> > Other than needing the display the IPA characters correctly, I'm pretty
> > sure this is just a matter of tying a database with fields for the
> > "question" and for the IPA characters to the page. I have no idea how
> > this is done, but I assume I can learn it.
> 
> Most definitely.  Any way you like from Perl/CGI to Ruby on Rails.

I have to learn a programming language? Can't I just get an app where I
can poke at stuff until it works? Colin's suggestion of WebGUI sounded
pretty good.

> > However, I also want to make a similar page where the "question" is a
> > sound file, using the same IPA character drop-downs as above. This may
> > be a bigger problem. I know it is possible to have sounds on a website
> > -- there are lots of them out there that I have been using. However,
> > would it be possible to tie several hundred sound files in a database
> > for the questions, so that the sound files would be presented
> > randomly?
> 
> Yup.  Databases can hold anything, including pathnames to the
> soundfiles, or the soundfiles themselves.  Might want to have a
> configuration thingy at the beginning to choose what sort of soundfiles
> to use.  (I.e. "Click here.  If that doesn't work, click here.")
> 
> Ever since I made an ugly voicemail-retrieval webpage for an Asterisk
> server, I've been looking for good examples of pages with clickable
> cross-platform sounds.  If you run across any, lemme know.

That brings up another question. Turns out I not only know next to
nothing about website programming, I also know little about sound file
formats. Assuming I want to be www compliant, and I want to support IE,
Firefox, Opera and Safari and the three OSs, MacOS, Linux and Windows,
what would be the most compatible sound format?

On Wed, Nov 22, 2006 at 02:32:36PM -0800, John Jason Jordan wrote:
 
> I tried out the demo, but it wouldn't run on Firefox. Undaunted, I
> tried Opera, and discovered that it wouldn't run on Opera either. But
> at least Opera told me why -- it needs the latest Flash plugin. Most

The flash demo is just eye candy.  It doesn't do anything useful
except show pieces of WebGUI with their icons.

Just log in as Admin, delete the flash demo, and add a Survey.  Then
tinker with it.

Oh, with respect to your other post, to extend the Survey module
you will have to know perl.  But it would be easier to find someone
to tweak an existing piece of software than to write one from the
ground up.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Wed Nov 22 15:06:44 2006
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Wed, 22 Nov 2006 15:06:44 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] remapping port with iptables
Message-ID: <20061122230643.GA14683@gate.kl-ic.com>


I have a 2.6.X server with a static IP address, connected directly
to the net, and running iptables.  It does not do NAT.  I am running
an application as nobody, and want to connect to a privileged tcp port
( port number < 1023 ) so I can get there through firewalls with 
restrictions.  One way for my app to be able to reconnect to the
privileged port is to remap it to an unprivileged port > 1024 .

Lets say I want to map the external port 321 on eth0 to port 3456
where the application can grab it.  I think it is possible to do
this with NAT rules and IPTABLES.  But I am not an iptables expert,
and could use some review and some confidence building.  Who can help?

If I understand correctly, there are two actions needed: (1) a PREROUTING
rule that does "destination NAT" and rewrites packets with destination
address 321 into packets with destination address 3456.  Next, (2) a
POSTROUTING rule that does "source NAT" and rewrites packets with
source address 3456 into packets with source address 321.  Perhaps
these two rules:

iptables -t nat -A  PREROUTING -d $ETH0 -p tcp --dport  321 -j DNAT \
         --to-destination $ETH0:3456
iptables -t nat -A POSTROUTING -s $ETH0 -p tcp --sport 3456 -j SNAT \
         --to-source $ETH0:321

... along with some magic line of stuff for my iptables rules (what?)
that figures out what $ETH0 is (the ip address of eth0).

What I am not sure about is what happens when an external client connects
to another application on my server, and my server assigns a port number
to complete the connection the other way.  Is iptables smart enough to
tell the application or the kernel not to use 3456?

Any suggestions, oh iptables wise ones?

Keith

From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Wed Nov 22 17:10:21 2006
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Wed, 22 Nov 2006 18:10:21 -0700 (MST)
Subject: [PLUG] Web site programming
In-Reply-To: <20061122141751.d0b97904.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20061122105316.0fd4f524.johnxj@comcast.net>
	<20061122200353.GA8121@syrinxpc.com>
	<20061122141751.d0b97904.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0611221732160.21574@sphinktoo.pippiandcarlos.com>

>> It's also possible to make the GIF/IPA thing configurable; maybe there's
>> even a reliable way to ask the browser if it can display IPA.

Here's one possible solution.  Examine the Accept-Charset HTTP header
and look for utf-8 support.  Here's a wireshark packet trace of a GET
request:

GET http://www.arts.gla.ac.uk/IPA/ipafonts.html HTTP/1.1
Host: www.arts.gla.ac.uk
User-Agent: Mozilla/5.0 (X11; U; Linux i686 (x86_64); en-US; rv:1.8.0.8) Gecko/20061025 Firefox/1.5.0.8
Accept: text/xml,application/xml,application/xhtml+xml,text/html;q=0.9,text/plain;q=0.8,image/png,*/*;q=0.5
Accept-Language: en-us,en;q=0.5
Accept-Encoding: gzip,deflate
Accept-Charset: ISO-8859-1,utf-8;q=0.7,*;q=0.7
Keep-Alive: 300
Proxy-Connection: keep-alive
Referer: http://www.arts.gla.ac.uk/IPA/ipa.html
If-Modified-Since: Wed, 15 Mar 2006 09:45:11 GMT
If-None-Match: "52b745271548c61:9c3"
Cache-Control: max-age=0

If a client supports UTF-8, then give him a page in which the
Content-Type is set:

<META http-equiv="Content-Type" content="text/html; charset=UTF-8"/>

Note: if you are sending XML pages to the client rather than HTML
pages, change the MIME type accordingly to text/xml.  I would use
UTF-8 for both the XML and the XSLT pages in this case.  But it's
unlikely you'll be going that route if you're not doing web
development as a paying gig.  Only paying gigs will go through that
much trouble to produce a web page.

Here's some links:

http://depts.washington.edu/llc/help/presentations/unicode_ipa/1_introduction.html
http://www.cl.cam.ac.uk/~mgk25/unicode.html

Keep in mind that unicode is a mapping of integers to characters (a
"character set"), while UTF-8 is a way to express those integers as
bytes in a computer file (a "character encoding").  The IPA characters
are part of the unicode character set (or so I think I have read),
which implies that they will not change from computer to computer,
since the unicode character set is wide enough to have a unique code
for every character on earth (or so they claim).  With UCS-4, a
4-byte-wide unicode character set, this is believable.

Carlos Konstanski

test drive.

> >
> > I want to untar jim and put it in /usr/local/bin

> Using GNU tar (which is probably what you have), it's easy
>
>   tar -x -C /usr/local/bin -f /usr/local/src/jim.tar

From tkubaska at charter.net  Tue Nov 28 10:45:10 2006
From: tkubaska at charter.net (tkubaska at charter.net)
Date: Tue, 28 Nov 2006 10:45:10 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] web site navigation map
Message-ID: <596193188.1164739510229.JavaMail.root@fepweb06>

Is it possible to automatically build a map of web navigation for a web site. . For a simple web site, you can just try out all the links and write down where they go, but for a large complex site this is impractical. I've searched around the net and so far have not been successful; possibly I'm mlooking for the wrong keywords.

What I mean is generating something like the following ('m calling it a web site navigation map; is there a better term?). What I've written below is strictly hierarchical, but there certainly can be backward links or links skipping a level.

Home page
   Link1 (goes to PageLink1)
      SubLink1 (a link on PageLink1; goes to PageSubLink1)
         SubSubLink1 (a link on PageSubLink1...
         .
         .
   Link2
       SubLink2
   Link3

 -Ted


From heinlein at madboa.com  Tue Nov 28 11:20:32 2006
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Tue, 28 Nov 2006 11:20:32 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] web site navigation map
In-Reply-To: <596193188.1164739510229.JavaMail.root@fepweb06>
References: <596193188.1164739510229.JavaMail.root@fepweb06>
Message-ID: <Pine.OSX.4.64.0611281107130.23262@arrowhead.galois.com>

On Tue, 28 Nov 2006, tkubaska at charter.net wrote:

> Is it possible to automatically build a map of web navigation for a 
> web site. . For a simple web site, you can just try out all the 
> links and write down where they go, but for a large complex site 
> this is impractical. I've searched around the net and so far have 
> not been successful; possibly I'm mlooking for the wrong keywords.

If all your HTML pages live in a filesystem, it's easy to 
conceptualize a simple program that would

 1. walk the filesystem,

 2. grab page titles and relative locations from the HTML
    files it encounters,

 3. build a PHP or SSI snippet containing a nav menu (referenced
    by each of the HTML pages containing a nav menu).

There are just too many snags for this to be practical:

 1. Menu text is usually a much shorter version of the target
    page's actual <title>...</title> text.

 2. What about pages you want to appear is multiple contexts?
    (OK, I guess symlinks would work, but it's kludgy...)

 3. What about HTML pages that you don't want to show up in
    a menu?

 4. What about automatically generated pages that don't appear
    in the filesystem or that appear via some CGI, Alias or
    Rewrite magic?

Additionally, my opinion is that the best menus are context-sensitive: 
they show you where you are in the navigational hierarchy and expand 
only that section of the site; other sections are represented in the 
menu only by their top-level entries.

For my money, it's best to develop a way to store and poll metadata 
about your site's page hierarchy. It could be a static PHP or SSI 
file, a simple structured text file, a semi-simple XML file, or data 
stored in a RDBMS.

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com


From tkubaska at charter.net  Tue Nov 28 12:12:37 2006
From: tkubaska at charter.net (tkubaska at charter.net)
Date: Tue, 28 Nov 2006 12:12:37 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] web site navigation map
Message-ID: <2129782461.1164744757217.JavaMail.root@fepweb09>

Thanks for responding. You are correct; a context sensitive menu is  best. My desire for a web site navigation map, though, is not so that I can put it on the web site.

I am faced with a large complex site with literally hundreds of pages and yes, some are automatically generated, I believe. I want to improve/refactor the navigation. What I was hoping for was a tool that would walk the site for me and give me a list of everything that was available and how you get there. The how is not its location in the file system but rather what you have to do in your browser to get there, following the rules of the site (that is, not typing in a url in the address bar). You know, going down a navigation tree until you reach a leaf and then back up and down another.

I wanted this map so that I could see the current situation before I muck it up. 

For a simple site, one can just click around and draw such a map in your notebook. But for a large complex site, this is very impractical.

 -Ted

From jvancleve at chrome.com  Tue Nov 28 12:57:42 2006
From: jvancleve at chrome.com (Jason Van Cleve)
Date: Tue, 28 Nov 2006 12:57:42 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] web site navigation map
Message-ID: <B5FE13D82C10EF4085844A9F70364A4F0BDF7A3E@PDXMSX02.hq.chrome.com>

Have you looked at the wget tool?  It has a recursive option for grabbing an
entire site (depending on how its navigation works).  That would download
all the pages in HTML form, as they appear to browsers.  At that point, you
might write something to parse through the pages and look for <a> tags,
building the map as it goes.

On the other hand, if you find a tool that will do all this for you, let us
know!

--Jason

are mostly for finding broken links and such, but they may prove useful in
your case.  Here's a starting point:

http://crawler.archive.org/

Thanks to those who responded about my request for sitemap generation. I downloaded and used Heritrix and Sitemapper. I haven't got the information I want out of eilther but this is definitely a start. The jargon seems to be "web crawler" for what I'm looking for. scrolling xterms.  I see no reason to waste power and money on
graphics capabilities I don't need.

From pm at nellump.net  Wed Nov 29 20:52:03 2006
From: pm at nellump.net (Paul Mullen)
Date: Wed, 29 Nov 2006 20:52:03 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Low Power DVI video cards?
In-Reply-To: <20061129222804.GA20628@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <20061129222804.GA20628@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <20061130045203.GA11508@nellump.ath.cx>

On Wed, Nov 29, 2006 at 02:28:04PM -0800, Keith Lofstrom wrote:
> Any suggestions for low power DVI video cards (with Linux drivers,
> of course)?

Between 'man radeon' and Wikipedia's "Radeon R200" entry, you ought to
be able to pick a suitable older ATi-based card. The latest that is
fully supported by the X.Org drivers (meaning 3-D and all that good
stuff) is the 9200-something, IIRC. Newer cards are useable with
binary ATi-supplied drivers (if you can sleep with yourself after
stooping so low). Most cards in the Radeon 9xxx range can be purchased
brand new for $20-40.

<http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radeon_R200>

-- 
Paul

After much fretting and research, I finally cut the Gordian Knot by
going to the Free Geek store.  Rick poked around and found 3 DVI
cards, and I bought all three for $10 each.  One worked easily.

Currently installed in the system was a Matrox G400 dual head card,
2 VGA, of which I was using only one.  Rick sold me these three
cards:

1) A Matrox G400 dual head with a DVI riser board.  It booted the
text startup of Fedora 1, but it would not go into graphics mode.
redhat-config-xfree86 (I am using legacy Fedora Core 1) failed to
talk to it, and I gave up after futzing around with the Xconfig
for a while.  Rather than waste time, I instead decided to try:

2) A Matrox G550.  This configured into X, but would not properly
configure for 1400x1050 to match my screen;  instead it interpolated
to 1280x1024 (which looks awful).  Rather than waste time, I tried:

3) A Radeon 7000/VE RV100 card.  This configured properly, and now
I am looking at 1400x1050 LCD with DVI drive.  Looks wonderful.
Admittedly, the VGA was not too bad, but I plan to connect the VGA
off my new LCD to the VGA switch box and get rid of another monitor,
saving a few millisalmon per week of electrical energy.

So thanks for all the suggestions.  I have some spare DVI boards
now, perhaps I will eventually find a DVI KVM switch box and learn
how to make these other boards work with it, replacing all the
VGA boards on the current KVM. 

And the main shopping suggestion is that Free Geek has a mess of
inexpensive video boards.  Do your holiday shopping there!

Keith

From johnxj at comcast.net  Thu Nov 30 20:02:04 2006
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Thu, 30 Nov 2006 20:02:04 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Bad-mouthing ext3
Message-ID: <20061130200204.991489d1.johnxj@comcast.net>

Several times I have complained here about ext3. It turns out that I
may owe ext3 an apology. 

The other day I was at PSU and my computer would not boot. The Compaq
splash screen came up, then nothing. Of course, this happened when I
had a presentation due in four hours and the Impress file was on the
computer. And I have to run it from the computer through the AV stuff
in the classroom because if I try to run an OOo file from my H drive it
would not likely work. I doubt that the university has OOo available on
the computers in the classroom, let alone the IPA fonts I needed or the
sound clips I had links to in the Impress file.

The help desk in the basement of Smith could not help, but suggested
that the CAT people in the CompSci building could probably assist a
Linux user. On the way to the CompSci building it dawned on me that if
Grub was not coming up, this must be a symptom that the hard drive was
not found. You'd think the BIOS would have the common sense to explain
this to me instead of just hanging, but we're talking cheap HP hardware
here.

When I got to the CompSci building the first thing I asked for was a
small phillips head screwdriver so I could reseat the drive. Believe it
or not, in the entire place no one could come up with a small phillips
head screwdriver. 

Back to the basement of Smith, the help desk people ushered me into the
back room where students were busy putting electronic things together.
The jefe running the place asked a student to hand me a small phillips
head screwdriver, whereupon the student handed me a slot head
screwdriver. There ensued a discussion between the jefe and the student
about the difference between phillips head and slot head (I am not
making this up), at the conclusion of which I finally secured the loan
of a small phillips head screwdriver.

Two minutes later my computer was happily booting into Ubuntu.

The point is that the continual corruption I have been experiencing may
have nothing to do with ext3. 


From ehem at m5p.com  Thu Nov 30 20:10:07 2006
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Thu, 30 Nov 2006 20:10:07 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Low Power DVI video cards?
In-Reply-To: <20061130200007.00F9F4175@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200612010410.kB14A7MH034954@m5p.com>

>From: Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com>
> I've got a new LCD monitor.  It looks OK with VGA 15 pin, but it
> would look better with DVI .  Sadly, the video cards in the stores
> are mostly fast-rendering game engines, which burn lots of electrical
> power.  The few low-cost low-power cards I've seen are VGA only.
> 
> Any suggestions for low power DVI video cards (with Linux drivers,
> of course)?  The fastest thing the card is going to show is fast
> scrolling xterms.  I see no reason to waste power and money on
> graphics capabilities I don't need.

I'm a bit surprised, I would of expected AGP vs PCI-E to be more of a
problem than low-end not having DVI. Nearly any card with less than 4 in
the hundreds place of the designation will be a low-power card. The
card number is typically GLRR. Where G is the generation (X1 is current
for ATi, 8 is just out for nVIDIA); the Radeon X are where ATi moved to
PCI-E, for nVIDIA it was GeForce 6, though AGP versions of those are
available. L is level, 8 or 9 for high-end, 5-7 for mid, 0-3 for low-end;
RR is either 00, or 50 for a revised version of some sort.

nVIDIA 5200 or 6200s are the nVIDIA ones you would of been looking for.

From plug_0 at robinson-west.com  Wed Nov 29 15:59:19 2006
From: plug_0 at robinson-west.com (someone)
Date: Wed, 29 Nov 2006 15:59:19 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Freedos discrimination...
Message-ID: <1164844760.4038.68.camel@goose.robinson-west.com>

What about dos makes it bad if you have a completely open source 
release of the once popular OS?

The ReactOS project in response to a question about Freedos's FAT32
support, "we won't use it because we already have our own support."
If the freedos FAT32 implementation and ReactOS's implementation aren't
compatible, how is that a good thing?  It is already frustrating to me
that Windows 98SE and Freedos 1.0 use incompatible FAT32
implementations.

If ReactOS becomes compatible with Microsoft's implementation of FAT
that has to be paid for but not Freedos's, how will that convince
anyone to replace Microsoft Windows with a free alternative? 

I for one want to see the ReactOS community get something out before
next year.  Their code audit still isn't done and they don't expect
to move their XP compatible offering into beta for quite some time.
Uhm, is it just me or is it getting a little late to achieve 
XP compatibility?  Vista will be pushing XP out for better or worse 
fairly soon.  Most likely worse.  Unless Microsoft finally flops a
Windows release, how is Vista going to do anything but hurt this open 
source Windows replacement effort?

<history_windows>

Let's look at the history of Windows briefly starting with 1995.

Between then and today, we have seen:

     Windows 95a
     Windows 95b
     Windows 95c

     Windows NT 4.0
     
     Windows 98
     Windows 98SE

     ??? Windows CE ???

     Windows ME

     Windows 2000

     Windows XP Home
     Windows XP Pro

     Windows 2003

     ??? Vista ( if it's out yet ) ???

At least 12 different releases of Windows where compatibility
was broken at least 3-4 times tracing from 95 to XP.  Windows NT 4.0,
2000, and 2003 are another path where compatibility has probably been
broken at least 3 more times.  This list is probably short and could be
expanded on the issue of compatibility by taking into account the side
effects of applying various service packs.  

Something to consider, activation became a requirement with the release
of Windows 2000 and Windows XP.  Activation adds a whole new source of
compatibility problems by exacerbating the OS obsolescence issue.  There
are companies that still use custom software written for Windows NT 4.0
that have no intention of porting it to 2000 or 2003.  If NT 4.0
required activation, they'd probably have to port their software today.
Porting can be expensive.  If a newer system doesn't offer anything you
want and what you have works, why port to it?  

As far as Windows CE, is it dead?  

Between 1995 and 2005 is a 10 year period.  This is the post dos era 
of Windows and I can't say that I am impressed.  As far as Vista, it
seems to be it's own beast.  It has a virgin network stack and
undoubtedly it breaks compatibility with both the NT and the 9x/XP line.
XP may be more properly classified as an ugly NT/9x hybrid release of
Windows than as part of the 9x line.  It seems that Millenium
was a 9x style system, but it wasn't compatible with 95 or 98 and it is
seemingly dead already.  I had Millenium on a Fred Meyer HP system for
about three days.  I took the system back because it blue screened and 
Redhat 6.2 couldn't support the integrated video card well.  I didn't
like that the system had a proprietary power supply either.

BTW:  Can I get the updates for anything that came before 
      Windows XP and Windows 2000/2003 on compact disc?
      Do any third parties put out Windows updates that 
      favor the Firefox/Thunderbird crowd?

      Is there a way to cheat XP/2xxx activation so I can reinstall
      from scratch long after Microsoft drops support?

      ReactOS is a legally safer alternative to cheating Windows 
      activation, but the project seems stalled and ReactOS definitely 
      isn't stable yet.  I've had ReactOS blue screen on me.

</history_windows>++
